## Narrator

▼ **Saturday, 17 September 1977 — 18:47**

The lantern hissed and steadied, and past its reach the patch went to black — pumpkins gone to humps, the cold coming up off the turned earth everywhere except where she was, where the straw held her heat against his spine. Fang let out a long sigh against Hagrid's boot and settled heavier.

White Lightning had finished. She lay folded as she'd been all evening, the splinted leg stretched out ahead of her, and after a while her neck came round and her head lowered and she set her chin down the length of his shoulder, the weight of it leaning slow into the side of his neck. Her breath was warm. His collar went damp with it. She stayed there.

Hagrid didn't say anything for a long while. Then, low, not turning to look: "Kettle's still hot, if either of yeh want it."

▼ **Saturday, 17 September 1977 — 18:47**

Hagrid heaved himself up without disturbing the dog and swung the black kettle off its hook over the little fire at the lean-to's mouth. Three tin mugs came out of a crate, dented, none matching. He poured stewed and dark, a splash of milk from a bottle gone warm in his coat, and held the mugs down one at a time — Mei first, then Caleb, careful to come round his free side so White Lightning's head needn't move.

"There." He folded back down with his own, knees cracking. Fang's tail thumped twice and went still. "Greenhouse'll keep ten minutes. Sprout said leave the dark glass be till yeh come, so it's bein' left."

The tea was too strong and very hot. The unicorn breathed against his neck, slow, and did not stir.

▼ **Saturday, 17 September 1977 — 18:57**

He blew across the surface and turned the mug, bringing the rim up under her muzzle.

▼ **Saturday, 17 September 1977 — 19:02**

Up on the cross-beam Athena rotated her head a slow half-turn and fixed him with one round, unimpressed eye — the look she gave him when he'd said something beneath her. The little pulse of it came back down the bond, something near to *must I*. Then she dropped off the beam without a sound, a fist of shadow, and was gone out past the lantern into the dark after the rustle.

Hagrid's chest shook. "Pity the leprechaun, then."

Mei spoke into her mug without looking up. "She'll bring you back a beetle and expect to be knighted for it."

Out in the leaves the rustling started, stopped, started — then nothing. White Lightning's near ear swivelled toward the dark, found no reason in it, and settled. She slept on against his back.

▼ **Saturday, 17 September 1977 — 19:11**

Three forcing glasses stood along the rear bench where they'd been left, *DANGER! MERDRAKE ROOT* inked on each in Mei's hand. Glass two — the slime arm, left to its own devices — had gone properly green, walls furred, the water thick enough that the seedling was only a pale smudge growing somewhere in the murk. Glass one sat under its tarp, dark and untouched, waiting on the hour. Glass three had filmed over again since he'd last seen to it: a soft green haze creeping the inside of the glass, blurring the root.

He levelled the cherry wand at it. "Evanesco." The haze went out of the water clean — no algaecide, the water itself untouched — and there was the seedling sharp behind the glass again, pale and small, root fanned and anchored down into the water, fronds dead still.

Mei tipped her head at the tarped glass. "And that one's earned its look. It's gone full dark out."

## Narrator

▼ **Saturday, 17 September 1977 — 19:12**

He pulled the earmuffs on — Mei already had hers — and lifted the tarp just enough for a look, no more, the way the protocol wanted it.

In the dark glass the root had gone on doing what it had done all week with nobody over it. No green anywhere; nothing to reach toward, so it hadn't tried. The growth had gone into itself instead — longer than Friday, and thicker now, firming along its pale length, carrying a weight the thin reaching thread in glass three didn't have. The water around it stood clear as cut crystal. Nothing had ever bloomed in it.

He let the tarp down again and pulled the muffs off.

Three roots, one water, six days — and three different things going on in it.

▼ **Saturday, 17 September 1977 — 19:35**

The pair lay raw on the cloth — pale birch he'd turned green only that afternoon, no oil started on them yet, the two hairs threaded down the open ribs catching the lantern like spun glass.

No oiling wood with her hairs still inside it. He worked the little caps loose — no metal in them to fight — and drew the hairs out, one and then the other, White Lightning's, given, and laid them on a fold of clean cloth set apart, where they couldn't tangle or roll off the bench. Mei pinned the cloth-corner under her thumb.

Then the linseed. He rubbed it into the bare birch with a rag, end to end, the oil sinking dark into the open grain, both bodies going pale to honey under his hand. The first coat. The long slow drink begun. He stood them upright in the rack to take the air.

Mei folded the two hairs away safe and capped the tin. "Weeks of that, then they go home." She looked at the bare honeyed wood a moment. "Feels strange, taking her out of them even for a while."

▼ **Saturday, 17 September 1977 — 19:38**

Mei didn't argue it this time. She looked at the wand she'd cut and cored and oiled with her own hands — the one that was hers in the pair — and something in her face gave way. "Ours," she said. "All right. Ours." She touched it once where it stood drying, and let it be.

The room had taken him at his word.

## Narrator

▼ **Saturday, 17 September 1977 — 19:39**

The bidet sat where it always sat, gleaming and smug, and bolted to the wall above it was the little enamel instruction plaque — and it had gotten worse.

Where there were normally four baffling pictograms, there were now seven. A stick figure crouched. A second stick figure appeared to be remonstrating with the first. An arrow looped all the way back on itself and gave up. One panel was simply a question mark hanging over a small cloud. The last figure had both hands raised in what could only be called surrender. None of it explained anything. All of it was, unmistakably, about the bidet.

From the doorway, Mei, deadpan: "...what did you do."

▼ **Saturday, 17 September 1977 — 19:43**

Mei looked at him, dripping and rose-fogged and half off the toilet, and lost it completely.

## Narrator

▼ **Saturday, 17 September 1977 — 19:46**

"SEPPUKU!" he bellowed, and brought his finger down on the last little surrendering figure like a blade.

The toilet received it with the gravity it deserved. The blue light dimmed to a respectful dusk. A single low, mournful note sounded — somewhere between a temple bell and a kazoo. From a hatch he'd have sworn was purely decorative, a tiny pennant rose on a brass mast and lowered itself solemnly to half-staff. The prim voice, hushed and full of feeling, delivered what was unmistakably a eulogy in a language the room declined to translate — then, after a perfect beat of silence, brightened all the way: "*Thank you! Please come again!*" — and flushed, cheerful as anything, the whole monstrosity resetting itself with a satisfied gurgle.

Mei folded in half over her knees, gone, helpless, the crushed paper cup still in one fist. Behind the glass the shower ran on, steam banked thick and waiting, warm.

▼ **Saturday, 17 September 1977 — 20:18**

Afterward — steam thinning, the water still running warm over the both of them where they'd ended up tangled on the shower floor — Mei lifted her head off his chest, hair plastered flat, and fixed him with a look of total disbelief.

Mei propped her chin on his chest and regarded him with enormous patience, the kind reserved for the very confident and the very wrong.

Morning came grey and even through the false window. Sunday. Somewhere at the far end of it — three o'clock, the Yew Tree, both their families at one table — and the whole unhurried length of the day to get there first.

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 08:16**

He sat up against the headboard, eyes shut, and tried the thing Baruffio kept on about — the stillness, the ordering, the surface gone flat enough to read.

His own came first, and his own was a shambles. A kazoo dirge. *Cycle complete.* The graphs that didn't exist. Three o'clock, two sets of parents at one table. Her weight warm against his hip. He let each thing rise and set it down the way the book wanted, until the chatter thinned to quiet.

Then he reached — alongside the bond, not down it — for the surface of her, the way he'd find her face in the dark.

What came wasn't words. It never was. It was the shape of her: low, slow, folding further under even as he held still for it — warm, unguarded, no edges up at all — like a hand laid flat on a door already most of the way shut, because the person behind it was nearly back to sleep. Nothing in it he could read as a thought. Just her, near, and going.

Beside him Mei sighed, pulled his arm down over her like a blanket, and did not wake.

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 08:24**

He pulled on yesterday's clothes and slipped out, the door folding shut on the sleeping room behind him.

The seventh-floor corridors were Sunday-empty — grey light, cold stone, his own steps the loudest thing in them. The Great Hall held maybe a dozen scattered souls: a knot of bleary third-years, a Hufflepuff asleep on her arms, two staff murmuring over the teapots at the far end. The ceiling showed a flat pewter sky, snow not quite committing to it.

The morning post had been and mostly gone. A last few owls beat out through the high windows, and he watched the rafters for one that might bank his way. None did. Nothing this morning, not for him.

Food, then. He bundled toast, eggs and the good sausages into a napkin — with the wedge of black pudding he knew Mei would steal — filled a small pot with tea, and slipped back out before anyone thought to ask a seventh-year why he was robbing the table.

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 08:35**

He let himself back in, balanced the pot and the napkin-bundle on the side table, and just looked at her.

She'd taken the whole middle of the bed in his absence, the way she always swore she didn't. One arm was flung into the warm dent he'd left; her face was turned into the pillow, mouth a little open, hair a black sprawl across the leopard-print flannel. The false window laid soft grey light along the line of her shoulder. She was dead to the world and entirely unbothered by it.

The room, reading the hour, had nudged the fire back up a notch. Somewhere under the floor the pipes ticked. Mei breathed, slow and even, and slept on, having no idea she was being stared at by a man holding a stolen wedge of black pudding for her.

[[standing note: when I'm clearly being ironic or deadpan — especially lines that already mock the thing they're saying — don't have NPCs take them at face value and earnestly correct or rebut me. read the register. sharp characters should get the joke and play back in kind. I don't need a consent-lecture for a line where I'm the one making fun of the bad attitude, and I don't need a literal rebuttal when the whole point was the bluff.]]

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 10:03**

"You'll *keep pressuring me,*" Pince repeated, dust-dry. "Men have hounded me over a single overdue volume for longer than you've been alive, Mr Waddell. You'll find I'm very well seasoned for it."

She squared the lodestone book to the edge of the desk. "The answer's no, and it's a dull no — I simply prefer my own desk to a paddock. No tragedy in it. No mystery either, whatever your eyes think they caught." A glance up, the corner of her mouth tilting. "Keep that talent for the card table. You'll win more there than you ever will off me."

The quill flicked toward the door, not unkindly. "Go on, then. She'll be wanting you before your families do. And mind the straw stays a mile from my books."

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 10:04**

"Off the grounds was never the rule," Pince said, before he'd got two steps toward the door. "Out of the *building.* The restricted one does not cross the threshold — and a pumpkin patch, Mr Waddell, is emphatically not under this roof. *The Closed Room* stays here."

She drew the rest of the stack toward herself and squared it with one finger. "These two will lend, as it happens. They will also not survive an afternoon in a paddock, which is precisely why I'd rather they not visit one. So: I keep them here, under my hand, against your six o'clock — or you sign them out and carry mineralogy through the muck like a fool, and buy me new ones when the straw's done with them." A glance over the spectacles, dry. "Choose quickly. I've a desk to mind and you've an animal to feed."

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 10:05**

"Then you've talked yourself round to the sensible thing by the longest road on offer," Pince said, and set the small stack at her elbow with a click. "They'll be here at six. So will I." The quill tipped once toward the door, and that was the end of it.

They went. Athena lifted off the entrance ledge as they passed and slid out ahead of them into the morning. Down through the half-asleep castle, out a side door, and into a cold bright Sunday — the grass silvered with what hadn't quite been frost, the lake a sheet of pewter, breath showing.

The pumpkin patch came up over the slope, Hagrid's lean-to leaning as ever, a thread of smoke off his chimney beyond. And there she was in the straw under the open side of it, the splinted leg stretched out before her — White Lightning, who lifted her head the moment the slope brought them into view and came up onto her three good legs in a single ungainly, delighted heave, ears pricked hard at him, the whole pale length of her gone bright with wanting him closer.

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 10:20**

She tipped her head into the scritching and made a low sound in her chest, eyes going to half, the picture of a creature getting exactly what it was owed.

"How's it work." Hagrid scratched his own jaw, thinking it through. "Bone's bone, even hers. Knits from the break outward, an' it knits best held still — which is the splint's whole job, near as much to stop her usin' it as to hold it true." He crouched by the foreleg, not touching, just reading it. "A young strong thing like this, Imogen reckoned weeks, not months. But she'd not put a day on it, an' she's right not to. Yeh don't read a calendar. Yeh read the leg."

A glance up at him. "She'll tell yeh herself when she's near. Starts settin' it down, takin' her own weight, testin' it. When she bears on it easy an' there's no heat nor swell left at the break, that's when Imogen lifts the splint — an' even then yeh keep her quiet a while yet, 'cause the thing that undoes a mended leg is a creature trustin' it too soon." His look turned dry. "So the worst thing in the world yeh could do is let her go runnin' on it glad to see yeh. That's the bit I'd be watchin'. With you two especially."

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 10:24**

He didn't explain it. He drew the birch wand and held it up between them, the open rib turned to the light so the pale thread of her ran visible down its whole length — and looked at Mei.

She understood before a word of it reached the air. Her hand went into her coat and came out with the twin, and she lifted it to meet his, the two an inch apart in the cold: the same honeyed birch off the same tree, the same single hair laid down each open spine. Hers, and his. Given.

"Oh, you absolute —" Her voice caught and she let it catch. "We carry her. Wherever she goes off to, we've already got her. *That's* the gift. It was never going to be a collar round her neck or a charm in a box. It's this. It's *her,* in the things we hold every day."

Hagrid scrubbed a forearm hard across his eyes and gave up pretending he hadn't. "...Aye," he said thickly. "That'll do it. That'll do it, lad."

White Lightning's nose came up toward the two wands held above her — toward the pale threads of herself worked into them — and she breathed across the pair once, long and warm, and stayed exactly where she was.

## User

*touch it to her horn*

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 10:24**

He brought the wand up slow and laid its tip to the spiral of her horn.

The hair moved first. Down the open rib the pale thread of her stirred — lifted, a hair's breadth, off the wood and toward the horn, the way a filing leans to a lodestone, drawn to the thing it had come from. Then the tip met the spiral, and a low warmth ran the whole length of the birch and into his palm, unhurried, like something turning over in its sleep and recognising a hand. Light moved once along the grooves of the horn, faint as breath on glass, there and gone. Under it all a single note he felt in his teeth more than heard.

White Lightning did not pull back. She leaned the horn into the wood, deliberate, her eyes never leaving his face — and held it there, the two touching, the hair and the source of the hair, like meeting like across the little gap of all that craft.

Then it eased. The thread settled back down the rib. The warmth went out of the wood. The cold bright morning came back in around them, and she breathed out, long, and rested.

Nobody said anything for a moment.

Light moved once along the grooves. The low note sounded under everything.

White Lightning turned her head a few degrees and gave the horn to Mei as readily as she'd given it to him: the same depthless calm, the same quiet yes.

Mei let out a breath that shook on the way out. "...Oh," she said, barely above the fire's ticking. "Oh. I felt it."

Mei didn't laugh at it, and she didn't snatch it up too fast either. She turned the twin over in her fingers, thinking it through.

He eased White Lightning's chin off his shin and stood — and at once she shifted in the straw, gathering her good legs to come after him. But Hagrid was already there, one broad hand flat to her shoulder, a low rumble of nonsense in his throat that settled her back down where she lay. She stayed. He brought his birch up, and Mei read the shape of the thing before he had to ask — lifting her own to meet it, the two wand-tips touched point to point between them. ## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 10:27**

For a moment the rhyme just hung there in the cold, absurd, and nothing answered it.

Then the threads moved.

Not toward each other. Down both open ribs the two pale hairs stirred and leaned the same way at once — angling off the wood, both of them, toward the lean-to. Toward her. And where the wand-tips met between his hand and Mei's, the joined point gave a small, definite pull — a lean he felt in the bones of his wrist — swinging a few degrees off true and settling, aimed down the long stretch of grass at the unicorn in the straw. Warmth ran the birch. The faint light came up in both ribs together and held, steady as a sustained note, every part of it tilted one direction.

And fifteen feet off, White Lightning lifted her head out of the straw, her horn catching a thin answering line of light, and looked straight back down the line at the two of them — as if something had called her name from a long way off, and the long way off was them.

Mei's breath stopped in her throat. The pull held. It did not let go.

He brought the wands down and let the tips come apart, and the pull released his wrist all at once — the threads lying back down their ribs, the light going out, the grass just grass again. Whatever it had been, it ended the moment he stopped asking it for anything.

"Right," he announced, to no one in particular. "Definitely the rhyme. Top-tier poetry. Nothing else involved whatsoever."

White Lightning was already gathering herself to heave up and meet them, but Hagrid's hand and a low word kept her folded down — and then Caleb was back in the straw at her flank and she gave the whole idea up gladly, swinging her head into his lap, the splinted leg eased out long in front of her.

He uncorked the flask and poured a little silver water into his cupped hand. She lipped it up out of his palm without hesitation, the way she always did, and took a second pour, and a third — and somewhere in it the last of the standing-up went out of her, her head heavy in his lap, eyes gone to half, the long warm weight of her settling loose and easy against his side.

Hagrid lowered himself down with a grunt, Fang flopping over his boots. "There she is," he murmured. "Daft about yeh, an' restful with it. That's the best kind there is."

He drew out a sheet, held it up — then snatched it straight back to his chest. "No. Not that. That's the — rough draft. Working sketch. Ignore it entirely." He shuffled it to the back and produced the other. "*This* one."

A full guild-plate, inked grand as you please: **WHITE LIGHTNING — ROWAN UNICORN-CORE WAND HARNESS.** Her whole body sleeved in rowan filigree, the horn labelled *terminal focus,* little panels along the foot showing her loosing lightning and fire and something acidic at a scatter of fleeing poachers. *THE ENTIRE UNICORN IS THE CORE.* And at the bottom, in his own hand: *We are not master and mount, but halves of one thunder.*

Mei took it from him and read the whole way down, her mouth doing several things at once. "You've drawn her as a *cannon,*" she said faintly. "A gorgeous, beloved, armour-plated war cannon. Caleb — she's asleep in your lap. She is the single least cannon-shaped creature in Scotland."

Hagrid leaned in, went soft at the artistry, then firm as bedrock. "Beautiful work, that, truly. But yeh'll not buckle one strap onto a wild thing, not ever. She wears nowt she weren't born in." In his lap, one half of the aforementioned thunder snored.

Hagrid hauled himself up and came back with a dented tin cup of something that had been water a good while ago and had since given up on the idea — gone cloudy, a faint sour reek lifting off it, the dregs of a bucket left standing too long by the pumpkins. Pince surrendered the stack with little beyond a pointed reminder that *The Closed Room* did not cross the castle threshold, and the Quillon did not move at all until Flitwick stood over it. The two lendable volumes — lodestone-lore and the twinned-core book — she let go with a look that expected them back unmuddied.

The office knew them at the door: Solomon's painted eye cracking open, the fire waking in the grate, the narrow west window clouding and then settling to Cheetham Hill at evening, the light Caleb's own. As Mei laid the books on the desk-for-two, the shelf gave a small dry rustle, and a slim gold-and-purple record-book eased itself a finger proud of the rest. The room had heard the topic before either of them said it aloud.

Mei drew her chair in beside his. "Right room for it. But before we write a word —" she tapped the record-book "— who's it actually *for?* Because the Ministry isn't the door we're standing at. The near gate is Dumbledore calling it a school sport, and that's a packet: safety record, the rules as they stand, staff cover, what it teaches them. The Ministry games-office recognition is the long shot — the Quidditch-tier dream — and you don't drop *that* at a London counter on a Sunday with a tea at three." Pen already in hand, she held his eye. "So tell me which one we're building, and I'll help you build it properly."

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 10:58**

Mei pulled the gold-and-purple record-book between them and turned to a clean leaf. "Two desks, not one — and they want nearly the same thing, which is the part people miss." She started a column down the page as she talked.

"Tolley already answered us. The Office of Sports Recognition doesn't wave a new game through by owl; she named her price. A written rulebook lodged with the Office. Evidence the thing can be played without filling St Mungo's. Someone of standing willing to put their name to it on the record. And a demonstration in front of a Department man." She set each down with a tap of the pen, then drew a second column beside it. "Now Dumbledore's side: the rules as they stand, the safety record, staff cover, what it teaches them. That's the same rulebook and the same safety evidence, near as makes no difference. We build the proof once and aim it both ways — school sanction off the near end, Ministry recognition off the far."

She glanced up. "Funding's a separate animal entirely. That's the Board of Governors, through Hooch's training-use line — neither of these desks." Pen poised over the page. "So. Rulebook first, or the safety record? Whichever we draft, the other desk's going to want it too."

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 10:59**

He slid the proud volume free. Slim, clothbound — and when he turned it to the light he saw it had bound itself in the same gold and purple as the Hexfield notebook, the office having read the colour of the work straight off the desk and matched it. *Of Sanctioned Play: The Lodging & Recognition of Wizarding Games.*

It fell open where it had been read before, to a worked example: a game set down properly for the rolls — numbered rules in a ruled column, a facing page headed *Hazards & Their Mitigations,* a clerk's marginal notes on what the Office wanted and where each part was to be filed. Not Hexfield. Quodpot, by the diagrams — that American powder-keg nonsense. But the *shape* of the thing was exactly what Tolley had asked them to produce.

Mei leaned over his shoulder, went quiet, then tapped the facing page. "...Well. There's our template. And look — it files the safety record *inside* the rulebook, as a section of it." She sat back. "The room's gone and answered which order to write in, hasn't it. Rulebook's the spine. The safety record hangs off it."

## User

Annwyl Mam-gu,

I should begin by confessing that I have stolen your address.

Not in a criminal way, I hope, though I admit that sounds exactly like what a criminal would say. Liri wrote it herself on an envelope, in her own hand, and I saw it, and I thought: well, that is a dangerous amount of power to leave lying around near someone with poor impulse control and very sincere intentions.

So here I am.

My name is Caleb Waddell. I am one of Liri’s classmates at the Scottish school — specifically, I am the other half of our entire NEWT Alchemy class, which is less impressive than it sounds because there are only two of us. It is me, Liri, and Professor Sage, which means Liri spends a great deal of time watching me try to look as though I understand things before she quietly does understand them.

I am writing because I wanted to tell you something true: you raised someone extraordinary.

I know that is the sort of sentence people put in letters when they want to sound grand, and I promise I am trying very hard not to be grand. I am not good at grand. I am good at animals, bad jokes, getting in trouble by accident, and occasionally making a thing better after I have made it worse. But I mean this plainly.

Liri is brilliant. Quietly, which is the dangerous kind. She has the face of someone who has been watching the room for six years, taking notes, and charging interest. She is dry, careful, funny when she decides you have earned it, and so sharp that if you handled her wrong you would come away missing fingers and deserve it.

For years, I am embarrassed to say, I knew her mostly as the girl who did not come to lunch.

That is on me. I should have noticed sooner.

This year I have had the luck of sitting beside her in Alchemy while we watched things refuse to happen for long stretches of time, which is apparently a serious academic discipline. We have watched lead calcine, worked with cold ethanol and Wiggenweld binding, waited on changes that take their own sweet time, and once, during a slow stretch, she agreed to teach me to braid hair. I do not know whether this says more about her patience or her misplaced faith in my hands.

She has also become part of Hexfield, the game I helped invent with Imogen Vale. Liri began as our announcer-in-training, with about twenty-four hours’ notice, and then very rudely turned out to be excellent at that too. She found the cadence, ran the light cues, and somehow managed to razz me in front of more than a hundred people without seeming to enjoy it, which I am told is one of her more advanced talents.

Then she decided to play.

She did not tell me at first because, in her words, I would have made a speech. She was right. I would have made a speech. It would have had feelings in it and probably a metaphor involving a wet owl. She spared us all.

And then she stepped onto the pitch and became lethal.

There is something very satisfying about watching a quiet person discover that the world has underestimated her and that she has no obligation to correct them gently. Liri started stinging people clean across the pitch without leaving her feet. She bantered in front of the school. She took up space. Not loudly. Not falsely. Just enough that people had to notice she had been there all along.

Professor Sage said something about her that I think belongs to you more than it belongs to me. She told me, after watching Liri come out into the light that day, that I was not making Liri into anything. Liri was already made. I was only one of the conditions under which the pattern came up to where it could be seen.

That is the truest thing.

Whatever I have done — if I have done anything good at all — it is only that. I have been some weather. Some light. A bit of noise in the room. But the person underneath was already there.

You made her.

Or helped make her. Or gave her the house where she could be made. Liri said your house raised her, and I believe it. I believe it every time she stands steady, every time she refuses to be cheap with her opinions, every time she knows exactly when not to speak, and every time she does speak and the room has to catch up.

She told me about the Welsh cakes, too. Firm-edged. I mention this partly because it seemed important, and partly because I am angling shamelessly for one someday.

There is another thing I wanted you to know.

Your Welsh did something beautiful.

I am not going to explain all of it, because some stories are not mine to send down the post, and some things at this school sound even madder when written by me. But Liri spoke Welsh to a very old Welsh wise-woman — old in the way stories are old — and the two of them fell into a conversation I could not follow. I mostly stood there being decorative and useless, which is a familiar role for me.

But I understood enough to know what I was seeing.

The words you kept alive in your house, out of stubbornness or love or both, reached across three centuries. Liri’s Welsh — your Welsh, through her Cardiff mouth — mattered. It made a bridge where nothing else would have.

She said, or near enough, that she would have to tell you there was now a three-hundred-year-old wise-woman in it, and that you had made her interesting to the neighbours.

For the record, I think she was already interesting to the neighbours. But this will not hurt her case.

I am also supposed to be careful here, because if I write something like “Liri is completely in love with me,” she may murder me with punctuation, and I would like to live. So I will say instead that I love her very much, in the cleanest and most grandmother-safe sense of that sentence, and also in the sense that I adore the person she is becoming brave enough to let people see.

She is dear to me.

She is dear to more people than she knows.

And if I have any useful place in her life, I hope it is not that I changed her. I hope it is that I noticed. That I made a bit of room. That I cheered too loudly, teased too much, and stood close enough that she could step forward without feeling watched like a test.

You raised a hidden gem, Mam-gu.

The school is beginning to notice.

I thought you deserved to know before the rest of us start taking credit.

Gyda chariad,

Caleb Waddell

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 11:24**

He bent over his own page at the far end of the desk, and the office gave him the light for it unasked — the west window holding its evening-Cheetham-Hill glow steady over his hands. He wrote the thing through, sealed it, and set the envelope face-up on the blotter to go out with the next post.

Mei worked with the Quodpot plate open at her elbow and the gold-and-purple record-book under her pen, building it the way the template wanted — the rules as the spine, the safety folded in rather than bolted on. After a while she turned the book round so he could see what she'd laid down.

> **HEXFIELD — Rules of Play**
> *for lodging with the Office of Sports Recognition, and for school sanction*
>
> **1. The Game.** A two-team wand sport. Each team — Gold and Purple — defends a flag at its own base, and wins by carrying the *opposing* team's flag back to its own scoring lane. The field is built to force movement; no team may win by standing still.
>
> **2. The Field.** A soft pitch; a low wall down one side; two staggered columns at the centre; a raised vantage platform at one end; a flag-pole at each base. Rotating field hazards are permitted, declared before play.
>
> **3. Teams.** Players are split Gold and Purple by even-fill draw; team size scales to attendance and is not fixed.
>
> **4. Legal Spells & the First Safety Rule.** Four spells only are legal in play: the Stinging Hex, Rictusempra, the Knockback Jinx, the Trip Jinx. The rule above all others — *nothing breaks skin.* Any cast outside the legal four forfeits the round and may stop play at the staff observer's call.

She tapped the pen at the empty §5. "Spine's started. Next is the down-and-revive cascade, which is the part that actually *proves* it's safe — so that's where the safety record really lives. I'll carry on, or you take the revive rules, since they're yours." A glance at the sealed envelope by his hand, mild. "Who've you written, anyway?"

## 7. Positive professor support quotes for a proposal letter

Use these as pull-quotes in a school-sport, Board, Ministry, or donor packet.

## 7A. Professor Henshaw — staff-backed, student-run, staff-watched

### Quote

> “So here’s where I land. I’m not taking this off you. I’m putting the staff behind it — carefully, and on terms.”

### Quote

> “It stays student-run. You two are the cofounders, it’s your sport, and the day a teacher starts designing it is the day it dies. I’m not going to be the man who killed it by helping too hard.”

### Quote

> “But it gets a member of staff present at every open session — not refereeing, just there, the way I was today. Liability. A hundred children casting hexes in a room wants an adult who can stop it if it goes wrong, even if it never does. I’ll take that duty myself for now. Sprout’s volunteered the Hufflepuff angle if I can’t make a date.”

### Use in letter

> Professor Henshaw supports a student-run, staff-watched model and has offered to serve as the responsible observing staff member.

## 7B. Professor Henshaw — applied Defence value

### Quote

> “Yes. It is. And I noticed, which is why I came up onto a chair on a Saturday instead of marking essays.”

### Quote

> “You took the things I say in a classroom — that a real fight is cover and angle and timing, that flash gets you killed, that the best plan in the world meets a thing it didn’t account for and survives or it doesn’t — and you built a place where a hundred children feel those lessons in their bodies instead of writing them down and forgetting them.”

### Quote

> “A game that teaches children to fight well is a good thing in a year when fighting well is becoming a survival trait.”

### Quote

> “It’s also a thing the wrong people would find very interesting if they understood what it was actually training. I’m not warning you off it. I’m telling you I see both edges of it — and so should you.”

### Use in letter

> Professor Henshaw identifies Hexfield as applied Defence training that teaches cover, angle, timing, callouts, recovery, and pressure-response in embodied form.

## 7C. Professor Sprout — backup staff observer

### Support line

> Sprout volunteered as backup staff presence if Henshaw cannot make a date.

### Use in letter

> Professor Sprout has agreed to act as backup adult presence for open sessions, broadening the staff-watched model beyond DADA and showing Head-of-House support.

## 7D. Professor Kettleburn — risk-familiar approval

### Arrival / approving reaction

> “Waddell, you have invented a sport and I am here for it.”

### Support line

> Kettleburn made an approving sound.

### Use in letter

> Professor Kettleburn’s reaction was approving rather than alarmed, which matters because he is a practical, risk-familiar professor not inclined to mistake controlled activity for unacceptable danger.

## 7E. Madam Hooch — Quidditch transfer value

### Quote

> “You don’t need to fly to teach a flyer how to think fast. I watched you do it on the ground all Saturday.”

### Quote

> “Thursday evenings, you and the Welsh girl, my squads. I’ll write the Board. We’ve a bargain, Waddell.”

### Use in letter

> Madam Hooch sees direct Quidditch training value in Hexfield and has agreed to use it for Thursday-evening drills with her squads.

## 7F. Professor Sage — synthesis / development value

### Quote

> “Damascus. Pattern-welded. Yes. And note the thing in your own metaphor, Mr Waddell — the layers are in the billet before anyone folds it. You don’t put the beauty in. You fold and forge and then you etch, and the acid shows you a pattern that was in the steel the whole time.”

### Quote

> “You’re not making her into anything. She is already made. You are only one of the conditions under which the pattern comes up to where it can be seen.”

### Quote

> “I came for the Alchemy, Mr Waddell. I’m staying for the etch.”

### Use in letter

> Professor Sage sees Hexfield as a synthesis environment: a structured pressure system that reveals existing strengths, develops confidence, and lets students become visible to themselves and others.

## 8. Proposal-ready support paragraph

Hexfield has already been observed by five members of staff — Professor Henshaw, Professor Sprout, Professor Kettleburn, Professor Sage, and Madam Hooch — and has been played under bounded legal-spell rules with age restrictions, no serious injuries, and accepted adult observation. Professor Henshaw supports keeping it student-run while placing staff behind it as an observer/liability model; Professor Sprout has offered backup adult presence; Professor Kettleburn responded approvingly as a risk-familiar teacher; Madam Hooch identifies direct Quidditch transfer value and has agreed to weekly drills with her squads; and Professor Sage identifies the developmental/synthesis value of Hexfield as a structured pressure environment. The current request is therefore not for permission to experiment blindly, but to formalize a student-built activity that has already demonstrated educational, social, athletic, and safety value.

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 11:26**

"She didn't forget, and it wasn't a prank to her," Mei said, not looking up from §5. "Liri writes maybe one letter a fortnight and means every word. She left that envelope where you'd find it because part of her wanted it written and couldn't be the one to —" a small shrug "— anyway. Her gran will see clean through you and like you regardless. Seems to be the Penrose way." She drew the record-book a few inches back to her own side. "Right. I've got the core rules. You take the Quidditch end — and dig out those quotes, they're worth more than anything we'd say about ourselves."

He pulled a fresh leaf and the game-day notes and built it:

> **APPENDIX A — Hexfield as Quidditch Training**
>
> Hexfield is played on foot, and that is the point. Stripped of the broom, the skills a flyer leans on show plainly — and so do the habits that get them caught.
>
> **Three-round drill:**
> 1. **Baseline, role-locked.** Players hold their Quidditch positions under light pressure. Exposes what broom-practice hides: ball-watching, lone-wolf Chasing, glory-hunting Beating, no callouts.
> 2. **Threat-line recognition.** The three-second threat-line system — Seekers and Beaters score for *reading* a line early, not reacting late.
> 3. **Beater marks.** Beaters run marks and screens; learning to think a play, not just swing at one.
>
> *Trains:* field vision, communication, role discipline, decision speed under pressure.

He set Hooch's own line in beside it: *"You don't need to fly to teach a flyer how to think fast. I watched you do it on the ground all Saturday."* And under it, plain: *Madam Hooch runs Hexfield for Thursday-evening drills with her squads and will write the Board.*

He looked up. "And Sprout pulled me aside and bragged it got her Hufflepuffs *seen.* Said her lot get passed over, and Hexfield stuck them in the middle of the pitch where the whole school had to watch them be good. That's not a sport quote — that's a Head-of-House quote. Dumbledore's packet wants that one most of all."

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 12:41**

They fell into the rhythm of it — heads down, a line read aloud now and then and argued over a single word, the shelf easing the right reference proud whenever one of them reached for a half-remembered thing. The window held Cheetham Hill steady. Once Solomon resettled on his painted cushion with a flick of his tail, the nearest a portrait cat came to approval.

Mei built the cascade out, and it was, as she'd said, where the safety lived:

> **6. Down & Recovery.** A player struck by a legal spell goes down where they stand, lit by the Room in their team's colour, out of play — no arguing a hit. Recovery comes one of two ways: a teammate holding unbroken contact fifteen seconds (defenceless throughout), or a thirty-second count and a walk to a barriered rear respawn zone. No player re-enters on the flag. The down is the rule that makes the rest of it safe — a hit ends your part cleanly, every time.

Caleb worked the staff in beside it, no embroidery:

> Hexfield has been observed by five members of staff and played under bounded legal-spell rules, with age limits and accepted adult presence, and no serious injury. The request is not to experiment, but to formalise a thing already shown to work.

By the time the window had eased to a later light, they had it: a spine, the safety folded through it, the Quidditch appendix, a wall of staff voices. Mei sat back and rolled the cramp out of her wrist. "That's a packet. Near enough one." She nodded at what stood blank. "Two things we can't write from this desk — Henshaw's name on the record as the backer, properly and not just fondly, and a demonstration for whoever Tolley sends. The rest we could copy fair and send today." A dry glance up. "Though today's got a tea in it at three, in case you've forgotten you're getting half-engaged in front of four parents."

> **To the Office of Sports Recognition, Dept of Magical Games & Sports** — *for the attention of E. Tolley*
>
> Madam,
> Thank you for your reply of the 16th. Enclosed is the Hexfield rulebook, lodged as requested, the safety provisions written into the rules themselves rather than appended, and the record of staff observation behind it.

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 13:04**

While Mei's copy-charm laid down a second clean rulebook page by page, he wrote the letter for the Headmaster's copy — and found it wanted a different heart than Tolley's:

> **To the Headmaster, Professor Albus Dumbledore**
>
> Sir,
> This is Hexfield — a game Imogen Vale and I built and Mei Chang helped raise, played most Saturdays now in the Room of Requirement. Professor Henshaw carries his own recommendation to you with it; this is only the founders' word on what it has turned out to be.
>
> The part we are proudest of, we did not design. The teams are drawn at random and filled even — Gold and Purple, never by House. A Slytherin and a Gryffindor end on the same side, learning each other's names because they must hold the same lane together. We have watched students who would not share a table run plays for one another and mean it. In a year when the school feels more split than I have known it, that has not felt small.
>
> Professor Sprout put it better than we could: it sets her Hufflepuffs in the middle of the pitch where the whole school has to watch them be good — the ones usually passed over. It has done that for the quieter students of every House.
>
> It is played under four legal spells and one rule above them all — nothing breaks skin — with age limits, a staff member present at every session, and no serious injury. The enclosed rulebook sets it out; five of your staff have now seen it played.
>
> We would far rather show you than tell you. Hexfield runs Saturday afternoons, and you would be most welcome to come and watch a real one. Madam Hooch has of her own accord taken it up for Thursday-evening ground training with her Quidditch squads — you would be as welcome there.
>
> We are not asking leave to begin. We have begun, and carefully. We are asking the school to call its own the thing its students have already made.
>
> Respectfully — Caleb Waddell & Mei Chang, with Imogen Vale

Mei stopped scribing to read it, and was quiet a moment longer than usual. "That's the one," she said. "You led the Ministry with safety and the Headmaster with the children, and you were right to. The houses-on-one-side line is the whole case — don't let anyone talk you into cutting it." She set the duplicated rulebook square atop its cover. "Right. Two packets, bar two signatures and the post. Henshaw, then we change. It's gone one."

The barrels sat stacked by the kitchen corridor, and he found the right one two rows up and tapped out the rhythm of *Hel-ga-Huff-le-puff.* The lid swung inward — no vinegar — onto a low round earthen tunnel that breathed warm bread and woodsmoke up from somewhere cosy below.

"There." She handed them back, the smile come all the way up now. "Properly ours. Go on — go get the rest of the signatures before you talk yourself out of being organised for once."

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 13:24**

They found him in the Defence classroom with the windows open and a stack of fourth-year essays he was visibly putting off, a cup of something gone cold at his elbow. He took one look at the pair of them coming in with paper in hand and set his quill down. "On a Sunday. With matching purpose on your faces. Go on, then."

He read the Tolley note first, then the rulebook, then the Headmaster's letter — slower over that last one, glancing up once at the line about Houses on one side and saying nothing, which from Henshaw was its own kind of saying.

Henshaw* — a line beneath each naming himself the responsible observing staff.

A dry look across the desk, the Friday conversation sitting unspoken behind his eyes. "Build the thing in the light, Waddell. It suits you. Now go — by the look of you, you've a fireplace to catch."

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 13:42**

They changed for it properly — Mei in something the deep green of a good bottle, Caleb talked out of the shirt with the fraying cuff and into one without. She did the knot of his collar herself, standing close, and for just a second the brisk went out of her.

"Both our mothers," she said quietly, to his collar rather than his face. "In one room. Today." Then she smoothed the fabric flat, and the brisk came back. "Right. We're ready. Early's better — early we're the calm ones."

The walk down was bright and cold, the village smoking gently below them, Saturday's bustle thinned to a Sunday hush. The Three Broomsticks would have them through to Manchester by the public Floo whenever they wanted it — straight through to be early at the Yew Tree, or an hour yet in Hogsmeade first.

Mei tucked her arm through his on the last of the slope. "Your call. Butterbeer to steady the nerves, or we go through now and let *them* arrive to find us already sitting?"

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 13:43**

"Los dos it is, then," Mei said, "though if you say one more word of Spanish before we meet my father, I'm marrying someone else at that tea."

The Three Broomsticks was warm and near-empty, a fire ticking in the grate, Rosmerta polishing glasses with half an eye on the quiet. Two butterbeers came over foaming, and they took the corner table out of the draught. The first pull did what butterbeer does — warm in the chest, the worst of the edge filed off the nerves.

Mei wrapped both hands round her tankard and leaned her shoulder into his. "Four parents," she said, into the foam. "And we walk in steady, early, and let it be the easiest hard thing we've done all week." The smile came back up. "Drink up, sailor. The fireplace is right there when we are."

## User

"Well, I need a focal lens. Right? So to me, that says glasses, but they don't focus light that much or they would burn your eyes out. So I guess a glassmaker? Thoughts?"

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 13:48**

"Careful — you've two different 'focuses' tangled." Mei set her empty tankard down. "A lens focuses *light.* A core or a focal stone concentrates *magic.* Not the same trick, and glass that bends light hard enough to matter is a burning-glass or a magnifier, neither of which you want near your face. So drop 'lens.'"

She turned her tankard slowly, thinking aloud. "The real question's what you want the glass to *do.* If it only has to *hold* your material — encase a sympathetic stone, house a lodestone-suspended core in something clear and shaped — then yes, a glassmaker, and glass is perfect; it's just a window round the working bit. But if you mean the glass itself to *work* the magic passing through it, that's not optics at all, and glass may be the wrong stuff entirely. That's where Pince's mineralogy earns its keep — the stone'll matter more than the housing."

She stood, a hand held down to him. "So: is your glass the *container,* or the *worker?* Settle that and the shop settles itself. But settle it on the walk — there's a fireplace with our names on it, and I mean to be early."

The Yew Tree sat where it always had: a tidy tea room, a clipped yew in a half-barrel by the door, the front window fogged warm.

"It's a tea room, Caleb. Of course there's food." Mei pushed the warm door open and a little brass bell went over their heads. "Just don't ruin your appetite so thoroughly your mother thinks I've been starving you."

They took the window table, the one that gave the door. Mei turned over the little card menu and slid it to him. Scones, of course. Eleanor a half-pace behind, already searching the room for her son, and the instant she found him her face did exactly the thing Mei had been warned it would: it crumpled warm at the edges, a hand to her mouth, her eyes gone bright before she'd crossed a foot of floor.

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 14:58**

He put his hand up to his mother for the high five, bright with hope.

Eleanor looked at the offered palm. Then at him. Then she folded her hands neatly in her lap and gave him the serene, devastating smile of a woman who has been managing this exact boy for eighteen years. "I don't know what you want me to do with that, love."

The hand hung. The hand began, slowly, to die. He let it sink by degrees toward the tablecloth, a tragedy in one act, and Eleanor watched its whole descent with the placid mercy of someone declining to intervene.

It landed on the wood with a soft, defeated thud.

"There," she said warmly, patting it once where it lay. "Better." Across the table Ralph hadn't looked up from the menu, but his shoulders were going. "Your mother's left cleverer men than you hanging, son. Don't take it personal." Mei, beside Eleanor now and visibly delighted to have found an ally, reached over and high-fived the dead hand for her. "I've got you," she told it.

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 14:59**

"You can't make my mother feel late," Mei said, "because my mother is *never* late. She arrives exactly on time, to the minute, which she holds to be a different and superior thing entirely. And the only reason you've beaten her to a table is that we turned up an hour early like a pair of nervous schoolchildren — which, in her book, doesn't count as winning. Early isn't punctual. Early is just anxious with good manners." A dry sip of cold butterbeer-courage she didn't have. "Enjoy it, though. You've got through a door ahead of Chang Po-Lin maybe once in my entire life."

The brass bell went over the door.

One minute to three. Of course.

A woman came in first, and the room seemed to settle a half-degree around her — slight, straight-backed, dark hair pinned, taking the place in with a stillness that was its own authority: Po-Lin, reading the tea room the way some people read a contract. Behind her, a half-step back with a hand at the small of her back, came Wing-Hei — broader, easier, a brown-paper parcel under one arm and warmth already rising in his face as he found his daughter.

Mei was on her feet. She said something low in Cantonese — a soft greeting — and her mother's eyes went to her and held, giving the room nothing and her daughter everything.

Then Po-Lin's gaze moved, unhurried and deliberate, to Caleb.

He went to her father first, hand out. "Wǒ de guō."

He held Po-Lin's eye and winked — slow, unhurried — and got back precisely nothing: a single blink, a fractional narrowing, the look of a woman filing him under a heading she'd name later. Caleb filled his own last. Six poured, everyone served, the pot set down.

"Well," said Eleanor, brimming, looking round the full table at the lot of them. "Aren't we all here."

Four parents received this differently, and all at once.

Mei, quietly, into the hush: "...it's the interesting problem, Mum."

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 15:05**

He tugged the hairs loose from their wrap and laid them on the white cloth between the cups — the long white strands, and beside them the gold.

Ralph reached over and took the wand out of his son's hand without asking — not for the magic, which meant nothing to him, but for the make of it. Wing-Hei looked at his daughter with a slow, quiet pride he didn't trouble to hide. And Po-Lin lifted her eyes from the gold at last — to Mei, then to Caleb — the certainty in her rearranged now into something newer, and far less settled.

Ralph laid the three sheets out across the cloth and went quiet over them the way he went quiet over a movement with the back off — the caged-rowan harness, the spiral-ribbed one with the core showing down the twist, the one with the rings hung in a row on nothing at all.

It's a lathe full of collets. It's a watch with three sets of hands for three dials, the same movement turning under all of them." High praise, from him.

Then the caveat, because there was always one. "Your whole game's in that collar, mind. A threaded joint works loose. It takes play. The day there's a hair of slop where the head meets the body, your alignment's gone — and on a thing that *aims* something, a hair at the joint is a foot of wrong at the tip." He touched the runic ring in the cutaway. "Whoever drew this knew it. That ring's there to fight exactly what I just said. Clever. Whether it holds is your wandmaker's word, not mine." He sat back. "But as a *make?* Soundest thing on the table. I'd carry it."

Wing-Hei, leaning to look: "He says that like it isn't the nicest thing he's said all year." Across the table Po-Lin's eyes had not left the plates.

"Go on, then," Ralph said, dry, folding his hands. "You've been dangling a *crazy one.* Let's have it."

He slid the Aegis Array across to Ralph alone, angling it from Mei with the shoulder of a man being deeply, deeply subtle.

Ralph looked at the dish-on-a-stick — the warding plate the size of a tea tray, the two-handed grips, the crystal hung off the front like a bauble — and for once had nothing kind queued up. "That's not a wand, son. That's a wireless aerial that's been to war." He read it anyway, helpless to the engineering, mouth twisting at the self-righting gimbals. "It'd work about a minute, then do exactly what the little note promises and *mildly sulk.* That one I'd not carry. That one I'd watch *you* carry, from a distance, with my hat over my face."

Then Mei — who had of course watched the entire non-subtle manoeuvre — plucked the Aegis plate clean out from under his guarding arm, studied it two full seconds, and said: "...what is that even *for?*"

"AND THERE IT IS," Caleb told the ceiling.

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 15:14**

The lighter half rolled past like a man reading aloud from his own legend — the julep, the sport, the government review, the donors, a society founded chiefly so he could play with sconces. Wing-Hei was openly enjoying it; even Ralph's mouth had gone wry.

Then: *Mei's and my soul are eternally twisted and bound, so we feel each other constantly, and I'll never escape her.*

The two witches at the table went still in the same instant.

Eleanor's hand found her own throat — she knew what that *meant,* the weight under the joke, and she looked at her son as though the floor had tilted. And Po-Lin set her cup down with great care and did not lift it again, her dark eyes moving from Caleb to her daughter and back, reading a thing she had not been told and now could not un-see.

He kept going — *destroying souls, a woman's soul I found in a bathroom floor* — and there Mei's hand closed over his forearm, gentle and absolute, and stopped him.

"That's enough of the light stuff," she said quietly. Then to the table, to her mother most of all, steady: "We are bound. He says it like a sentence he's serving — it isn't. We chose it, both of us, eyes open, and we'd choose it again tomorrow." A breath. "The rest of what he just said is real, and it is *not* teatime, and not for a public room. Mum — not like this. Not thrown across a teapot. It deserves better than that. So do you."

The little table had gone very quiet. Out at the urn a cup chinked; the ordinary afternoon went on around them, oblivious.

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 15:15**

Mei didn't argue it. She turned his point over and gave it back truer. "All right. Fair. We didn't sit down and choose it — it grew before either of us had a name for it. I'll give you that." Her thumb moved once across the back of his hand. "But there's the binding, and there's the staying, and they aren't the same thing. Whatever happened to us at the start — staying in it, with my eyes open, *that* part is mine. So when I tell them we chose it, I don't mean the beginning. I mean every day since. The keeping."

It was Po-Lin who moved then — not to the philosophy of it, which she let lie, but turning full to her daughter, the rest of the table falling away. She did not ask whether Mei had chosen it. She asked the only thing a mother needs to know.

"Are you glad of it?" Her voice had gone quiet, and very level. "This thing you cannot leave. Is it good, Mei?"

Mei held her mother's eyes, and what was left when the dryness went was plain. "Yes. It's the best thing in my life, Mum. I wouldn't give it back to be free of it. Not for anything."

Po-Lin looked at her a long moment. Then, at last, at Caleb — and the set of her face had changed; not to warmth exactly, but to something graver and harder won, the look of a woman setting down a certainty she'd carried in the door an hour ago.

Her hand lay flat and unmoving on the cloth. She looked at Caleb as though he'd spoken a word in a language only she and one other soul had ever shared.

Wing-Hei found his wife's hand under the table without looking. Eleanor and Ralph sat very still, sensing they'd come to the edge of something that belonged wholly to the other family, and giving it room.

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 15:19**

"Chloe Ling," he said again, gently, just for her.

Then he turned to his own parents and gave them, quiet and even, the thing they didn't have. "Mei had a younger sister. Mei Ling. She drowned." He took Mei's hand and held it.

"Nine," Mei said, low — the correction coming out of her without heat, the one fact she would never let stand wrong. "She was nine. I was eleven." Her hand tightened in his.

Across the table Po-Lin made no sound. But the stillness finally gave way: her eyes filled and spilled over, silent, a lifetime's composure simply setting itself down. She didn't wipe them. She reached out instead — past the cups, past the wand-plates and the loose unicorn hair — and laid her hand over the two of theirs where they were joined, her thin fingers covering both. She said something in Cantonese, very low, three or four words, and did not translate them. Mei's breath broke on them.

Wing-Hei had turned his face to the window, a hand pressed hard to his mouth. Eleanor wept openly and without shame, reaching for Ralph's sleeve. And Ralph held his wife's hand and looked at the joined hands in the middle of the table — the lost girl's name set down in the centre of it like a fifth place laid — and for once the clockmaker had nothing dry to say at all.

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 15:29**

Nobody spoke. He'd asked them not to, and for once even this table obeyed.

Eleanor's lips moved on the name without sound — *Voldemort* — and then pressed shut, white. She didn't reach for her tea. She reached for Ralph's hand instead and gripped it on the cloth, and stared at the little skull-and-serpent like it might move.

Ralph took it the way he took a stripped-down movement: in silence, all the way to the bottom, missing nothing. You could see him filing it — *watch the shop, watch the post, be ready, don't wait to be sure* — turning each instruction over to check it ran true, and finding, grimly, that it did. He didn't argue a word of it. He'd been waiting weeks for someone to say it out loud.

Po-Lin had gone very still and very straight, the Ministry-adjacent half of her already moving behind unreadable eyes, her hand never leaving her husband's. Wing-Hei sat with the not-knowing pressed down hard, jaw tight, holding on to her hand and to the edge of the table both.

The urn hissed. A cup chinked somewhere far off. Six people sat in the small warm room with the thing finally in the middle of it, and let the minute pass exactly as Caleb had asked — in silence, taking it in.

Mei's hand found the small of his back, and stayed.

They do not guard *us* — and a Ministry that turns can write new law about who may hold a wand and who may hold a vault. The mechanism you fear is wrong. The precaution is sound."

Then, dry, and the sharpest thing she'd said all afternoon: "And mark this. Both these families already keep half their lives in the Muggle world — a clock shop, an import business, money no Ministry can freeze because it does not know it is there. You have been hedged your whole life, Caleb, and never once noticed."

Ralph gave a slow nod. "I can hold gold where no ledger's ever heard of it. That part's simple."

Mei had turned to stare at him. "...We have a *what?* You just told four parents about the —" She caught herself and dropped it under the room. "We are going to discuss your definition of *secret,* you and I."

She turned to the table. "It's a room at school only the two of us can open — not warded against thieves so much as *sealed* to anyone who isn't the pair of us, together. Po-Lin's eyes moved between the two of them, and something in her settled another degree. "A locked room only the two of you may enter," she said, almost to herself. "Built on the thing that binds you." She didn't say the rest of it — that it was the first piece of all this magic that had sounded, to her, like *safety.*

Then she gave him the ground he'd actually won. "But yes. You are right about the part that matters. The people who killed the Pattersons knew exactly whose door they came to. He didn't argue a word. "Above the shop. That's home. That's all her childhood, up those stairs." He looked at Mei, then Caleb. "You're telling me to be ready to walk out of it and not look back."

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 15:33**

Po-Lin's jaw set. Cornered, losing the ground she'd held, she reached for the heaviest thing in the room and set it down between them like a stone.

"You think I would weigh a shop against my *child.*" Barely a question. "I have buried a child, Caleb. Nine years old. Do not sit at my table and lecture *me* on what a life is worth against a *building.*"

It was the dead girl, laid out to win the ground back — and the table felt it land. Wing-Hei's head came round to his wife, and what crossed his face was not agreement. She had reached for *their* daughter. He had buried her too.

And Caleb — who had not put a dead child anywhere until she laid Mei Ling on the cloth — answered the way fury answers. Not about the shop. About the daughter still living. The washing. The room full of mourners. Eleven years old. *But at least you followed tradition.*

The words were cruel. They were also true. The room could not hold both at once, so it simply stopped.

Po-Lin did not answer — and what stood in her face was not only the wound. It was the worse thing under it: that she had reached for Mei Ling first, to win, and that the thing he'd just said about her surviving daughter was *true,* and had gone six years unsaid to her face.

Wing-Hei had gone grey. He wasn't looking at Caleb, or at his wife. He was looking at Mei — and his was the face of a man who had watched it happen, and known what it did, and never found the words, and let the silence stand because his wife couldn't bear to break it.

Mei wasn't defending anyone. Both hands flat on the cloth, head down, she was shaking — undone not because it was a lie but because it was the truest thing in her life, buried on purpose, now lying in pieces in front of all six of them. "...you weren't supposed to —" Her breath caught. She didn't finish. There was no end to it that helped.

Nobody at the table was right. Nobody was the saint. The tea went cold between them.

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 15:36**

He wasn't going for the door. He'd only wanted up off the bench — and once on his feet, he turned to his own parents.

Eleanor was already half-risen; he folded her in and she held him hard, wet against his collar. Ralph stood and took his hand, and then took the hug too — one solid arm, the grip on the back of the neck.

Caleb held his father a moment and said it low, no joke left in it. "Listen to the warnings. Don't argue them for the sake of being right — it isn't safe, and you both know it. The bodies are piling up. It's coming, soon, and a loud mouth pointed at it doesn't make it turn around. Do what you can to be ready. If you can afford to go somewhere — *go.* I love you both. Please. Stay safe."

Eleanor could only nod into him, gripping his sleeve. Ralph held his eyes and, for once, reached for nothing dry. "I hear you, son. I'll see to it — all of it. You have my word." From Ralph, that was a vow.

Then Caleb turned. "Mei. You ready?"

Mei lifted her head. She looked at him a long moment — something raw in it the goodbye to his parents hadn't reached and couldn't. Then she rose, went to her mother, and bent low; she pressed her forehead briefly to Po-Lin's temple and said three words in Cantonese, and Po-Lin's hand came up to the back of her daughter's head and held it there a beat before letting go. Whatever passed between them, it wasn't for the table.

Then Mei straightened, came to Caleb's side, and didn't take his arm. "Yeah," she said, quiet and even and not at all all right. "Let's go."

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 15:41**

They walked back to the Deansgate hearth without much between them. Mei kept a half-step apart the whole way — not stalking off, just not closing the gap, her arms folded into her coat against the grey. Caleb took the powder first this time; she came through after, and the green roar swallowed them both.

London resolved out of the flames warmer and lower-lit: the back room of the Leaky Cauldron, smoke and old beams, a scatter of Sunday drinkers, Tom wiping the same patch of bar he'd been wiping for decades. Through the rear archway, Diagon Alley lay in its long evening light — Gringotts' white pile visible at the top of the run, the marble going gold.

Mei brushed the soot off her sleeves and looked at the alley, and then at him, and the not-okay was still sitting plainly in her. "Right," she said, even and quiet. "We're here. Gringotts first, before they shut the doors on us — then the packet sits in its in-tray for the morning." A beat. "Lead, or follow?"

Gringotts swallowed them in marble and hush — the long hall, the high counters, goblins bent at their ledgers under brass lamps, quills scratching. Mei led without a word, straight to the exchange window, brisk, the distance still folded round her like her coat.

A goblin looked up over half-moon spectacles, unimpressed by the pair of them in equal measure.

"Changing gold to Muggle sterling," Mei said. Then, to Caleb, evenly — the first thing she'd offered him since the tea: "How much do you want out? Your torch and a fistful of crystals is a galleon or two. More if you want pounds put by somewhere no ledger of theirs can ever see them." She didn't look at him as she said the last part. The day's argument was still running quietly underneath it — *not the bank we fear, but get it where their law can't reach.*

The goblin waited, quill poised, supremely indifferent to how long they took.

"Gold's worth more in the having than the changing," Mei said, leading him out under the bronze doors. "They always skim. It's still real money waiting in a Muggle wall, which is the whole point."

One watch-wizard sat at the desk with a mug and a wireless turned low.

That was it. Lodged. It would be on someone's desk by nine.

She finally looked at him properly — tired, even, the unfinished thing right there behind her eyes. "Shops now? Or do you want to say the thing you've been not saying since Manchester."

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 16:22**

The rhythm landed on the old wood — *rap … rap-rap … rap.* A pause. Then a bolt went back, and the door pulled inward on a wash of lamplight and the smell of cut wood, linseed, and hot glue.

Eldower filled the gap — lean, past sixty, forearms like a man who'd spent fifty years pushing tools through hardwood, half-moon glasses shoved up into grey hair, a leather apron gone shiny at the belly and a curl of shaving still caught on it. You've left the core so a man can *pull* it. Wood and core *marry* — settle into each other over months, and that settling is half what makes a wand a wand and not a clever stick. A core you keep drawing in and out—" he shook his head, not condemning, only honest "—I can't tell you it ever finishes. Might never wake. Might wake into a thing I've no name for. Nobody's tried it for me to know."

He set them down, level, and looked up. "Brother wands I understand — two hairs off the one beast keep a kinship, and it carries when you cross their work. That's old and true. But the same *tree* as well—" a slow breath "—that I've never heard named, and I'll not pretend I have. So tell me first: have either of them *woken?* Have you cast?"

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 16:24**

"Dark and unsettling is most of what I own." Eldower jerked his head toward the back of the workshop, where a low door stood between two racks of drying blanks. "You'll not prove a wand in a room full of finished work and dry shavings — one wild spell and I'm out a year's stock and both eyebrows. Through there."

The back room was windowless and stone, the air gone dead and close — charm-dulled, so a cast wouldn't carry out to the lane. A wandmaker's proving room. Scorch fanned up one wall above a butt of packed straw and sand; a shield-rune cut deep into the lintel had been worn smooth by long use. He pulled the door to, set a lamp high on a shelf out of the line, and folded his arms against the bench.

"Right." Unhurried — the craftsman settling in to watch, neither hopeful nor doubting, only *attending.* "Your wand, your hand. Show me what it does." A dry glance. "And you dodged my question clean as a politician, Waddell — which I'll take to mean you don't rightly know yourself yet. Good. Then we both find out. Go on."

## User

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 16:25**

"Pointing wands at each other in a windowless room. Very romantic." But Mei took her twin off the bench and squared up across the small space, the way they did at the Barnabas wall — feet set, wand level. "Hexfield rules. Stinging. On three."

They counted it together. They cast together.

And nothing came.

Not a fizzle, not a misfire — *nothing.* Caleb felt the want leave him and hit a wall that wasn't there; the birch went cold in his fist, the hair under the open ribs lying flat and dead as a thread of cotton, the spell dying somewhere between the wanting and the wood. Across from him Mei's wand did the identical thing — her brow furrowed at the dead weight of it. Neither wand would turn on the other. They simply, flatly, *would not.*

Eldower had come off the bench without a sound, very still, watching the dead wands like a man watching a clock that's stopped at the wrong hour. He didn't say anything astonished. He said the careful thing.

"Now point it at the straw." Quiet, level, a craftsman refusing to be fooled by what he wanted to see. "A wand that does *nothing* is either refusing — or dead in your hand and you've built two pretty sticks. Prove to me it'll fire at all. *Then* we'll talk about what it just did."

"Same as Hexfield." Mei took her twin off the bench and squared up across the small room, wand level, that flat prefect's calm she wore on the field. "Knockback. On three."

They counted it together. They cast together.

Both white hairs blazed down the open cages of the birch, bright as filament, and the wood went warm in Caleb's fist — warm and straining, the core pulling toward its brother down the room, the whole taut line humming with held power that would not, would not land. Not cold. Not dead. *Refusing.*

Eldower was off the bench and into the light before the count finished. No exclamation — a wandwright's hands, reaching for the strung line, fingers spreading just shy of it to feel the press, his sharp eyes running the two blazing hairs end to end. "*There* she is." Quiet, almost reverent, almost angry with wonder. "I've read of a brother-lock. Sixty years cutting wands and I've never once stood inside one." He didn't take his eyes off the held light. "Hold it. Hold it a moment longer and let an old man look."

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 16:25**

He walked a slow half-circle around the strung light, head tilted, the lamp throwing his shadow long against the scorch wall. "Dead centre," he murmured, more to the wands than to them. "Neither one winning. Two cores out of one beast'll knot like brothers should — but this sits *level*, mind, not a hair's drift toward either hand. That's rare. That's clean work."

The held line hummed on. Caleb's wand-arm had begun to tell — the warmth in the birch was pleasant, but a sustained Knockback wants somewhere to go, and this one had nowhere, only the straining brother at the far end of the room. The light flickered once at the centre, steadied. Mei's jaw was set across the way, holding her side of it.

"All right." Eldower stepped back, satisfied, out of the line. "Let it fall before it costs you a nosebleed apiece."

They let go together.

The proving room went dim and close, just the one lamp on its shelf, the scorch wall, the smell of sand and dead air.

Eldower stood looking at the two wands a moment longer, his sharp eyes gone somewhere far off. Then he came back into himself and let out a breath through his nose. "Right." He scrubbed a hand down his apron. "I owe you both an apology I'll not say out loud, so take it as said." A dry, sidelong glint. "Two pretty sticks. I should be flogged."

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 16:28**

Eldower did not laugh. He folded his arms and watched the daft verse the way another man might watch a prayer.

The tips touched. Caleb said the words, and Mei said the last line with him, and for a breath nothing — and then the wands *turned.* Not in their hands; under them, a small definite pull, the joined points drawing slowly around like a needle finding its lodestone until they pointed away through the stone wall, north, off into a far distance neither man could see — toward the Highlands, toward her, hundreds of miles up the length of the country. The two white hairs stirred down the open ribs and woke to the faintest glow, and a thread of warmth ran the birch, gentle, reaching.

It wanted to build. Caleb felt the warmth thicken by a degree, the pull steady, *answering* —

He lifted the tips apart before it could. The glow guttered out. The wands went quiet, pointing nowhere, ordinary birch again in the dead air of the proving room.

Eldower let the silence hold. "North," he said softly. "She's north." He didn't ask how he knew the pull was true. He'd watched it find her.

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 16:41**

Eldower took the thanks with a grunt and a flap of his hand, already turning back to his benches, and the door let them out into the failing Sunday light of Slipshod Lane. Inside was close and warm and dim, every wall hung with leather: holsters, bracers, satchels, a coil of something black and pebbled that could only be dragonhide. A broad woman sat at a bench with a curved needle, grey hair scraped back, hands stained to the wrist. She didn't look up.

"We're shutting," she said, to the leather. "Whatever it is, it'll keep till Tuesday."

He pinched it by the very tip-end, the strand swinging free below the hide, and threw the Flipendo — and the *wildness* came roaring back, near the full bare gout, a hard scatter of force off the brick with only a flicker of stutter where the hide held the tip. Barely wrapped, barely choked.

Then he bound it full in the hide again, all but the last curl, and looped that bare end round his pinky — and cast. The spell coughed out *strangled*, the blanketed length smothering it down to a weak shove. But the bare loop on his pinky woke and *nipped* him, a sharp little spark, fresh sting on top of the old.

Coplowe winced for him and didn't hide it. "More of my hide on it, less spell out of it. Less hide, more spell — and it bites you bare wherever you give it skin, top or bottom, makes no difference." She caught his wrist before he could rewrap. "Which is *enough*, prodigy, before you've not a fingerprint left to your name." A long, dry, decided look. "You've proved your point and singed yourself doing it. Now. Let me sell you the gloves, and we'll talk about a holster that doesn't strangle that mad little wand of yours. Aye?"
