## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 16:50**

"Because they don't cast through the *glove*, lad — they cast through the *wand.*" Coplowe said it like a woman who'd answered it a hundred times. "My hide never goes near the working of it. Sits on the hand, the hand sits on the stick, the stick does the magic. I sell gloves to herbologists and dragon-men and folk who handle things that bite — for the *holding,* not the casting. You're the only fool I've met daft enough to grip the bare fire itself."

He proved her half-right and half-wrong at once. Gloved hand, the strand pinched in two fingertips — he cast, and his hand stayed whole, and the force came near full, only the two points of hide thieving a little off it.

Then, gloved, he fed the heartstring back down the rowan's open ribs, capped it, and threw the Flipendo at the brick — and *that* was the answer to everything. It fired hard and clean, the kick carried into the wood, channelled, aimed, his hand never touched by it. No bite. No strangle. Full.

Coplowe whistled low. "There. The wood *carries* it. My hide only ever *stops* it." She nodded, settled, craft to craft. "Wood for the casting, hide for the carrying. Now — gloves and a holster, before I die of old age?"

## User

[[so to clarify holding the wand with the glove is somehow different from casting through the core with my give?]]

## Narrator

[OOC: Fair question — you've put your finger on the actual seam, and it's worth being clear so you can play it accurately.

What the afternoon's *shown* (observable, in-world):

- **Bare core in your fist:** your grip and anything wrapped on it *are* the channel — there's no wand body, so the spell-turning hide sits right in the working path. Hence the big choke, and the choke scaling with coverage.
- **A finished wand gripped through a glove:** the wand (wood + core) is the channel; your gloved hand grips the *handle* from outside it. The two-fingertip test is the tell — contact hide on the core still thieved only "a little," because it was a thin touch, not a wrap. A glove on a wand handle is that same thin touch, and the wand carries the cast on past it.

So it's **degree + position**: one thin layer at the grip = negligible; hide interposed *along* a bare core's length = murder.

The *why* underneath — why an attuned wooden wand barely cares about a glove on its handle while a naked core cares about everything touching it — is exactly the thing Coplowe punts to Eldower and Eldower won't claim to know. That part's still yours to chase. I'm not going to hand you the law; the experiments are how you'll corner it.

Did I render anything inconsistent, or does that hold together for you?]

"Arcing Diffindo. A Protego that wraps." Coplowe huffed, almost admiring. "Off with you, prodigy, before you invent something I have to read about."

Then Muggle London swallowed them for half an hour. A hardware shop, fluorescent and ordinary, for a black torch with a head that twisted the beam tight or wide. Neither of them had the words left for the day they'd had, so they didn't try. The gates loomed, the wards humming their quiet recognition, and they were home.

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 19:05**

Three passes of the seventh-floor wall and the door came as a pair of soot-blackened smithy doors, heat already breathing out the seam.

Inside: a forge glowing orange, the coal-smell thick and good, two anvils squat and waiting side by side — one apiece. A rack of hammers and tongs, two leather aprons hung on a peg. At a third anvil stood a figure of no particular face, a smith-shaped thing in its own apron, patiently tapping a bar of hot iron — *drawing it out,* turn and tap, turn and tap, the simplest honest motion there is — then holding up the tapered result for them to see before laying down a fresh billet for each of them.

Mei was already shrugging into an apron, sleeves shoved up, that focused quiet coming over her she got at any bench. "Two bars," she said, deadpan, threading her hair back. "You planned this. You bought me a hobby." She hefted a hammer, testing the weight, unimpressed and entirely game. "Right. Show me how to hit something correctly for once today."

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 19:57**

Mei didn't chase him. She didn't say *it's not invented, I've lived with her thirty years* — he'd already named it invention, and there was no use spending it on a closing door. The corridor was dark and cold after the forge-heat. Seventh floor, the southwest turret stair, the small portrait of Solomon dozing in his painted window. The cat cracked one eye. *We have come from the bust,* and the canvas swung in.

The office was just as they'd left it that morning — books, the two chairs, the window still showing some grey scrap of Manchester sky out of habit. Empty. Quiet. And under the quiet, the bond did what it always did, unbidden: a low thread of *her,* somewhere off across the castle, the shape of her mood carrying faint and steady through the dark — not words, never words, just the weather of her. Tired. Out of sorts. Still there.

The day was finally, completely over. The room waited to see what he'd do with the end of it.

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 19:59**

His own churn he could press on — jaw set, breath held, the crude white-knuckled work of just refusing the feeling, shoving it down under the floor of himself. That much bent to him.

The bond did not. There was no taught door to it, no valve his hands had ever been shown — only the thread of her running steady through the dark, and him leaning his whole will against it like a man putting his shoulder to a wall of water. It dimmed. For a moment it thinned, the weather of her guttering low as a hand cupped over a candle-flame — and the cost came at once, pressure massing behind his eyes, an ache winding tight at his temples, the same toll the reaching always took, building toward something worse if he held it.

He held it. One breath. Two. And the seam would not seal. The instant his focus slipped a hair, she bled back in — faint, tired, unhurried, *still there,* impossible to lock out with a will that had never been trained to do it.

The candle-ache throbbed. The room was very quiet around the failing of it.

*I read the mind defense book that is there*

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 20:18**

Vavasour was where Mei had left it for him — squat, black, *The Closed Room* tooled small on the spine. He set Baruffio aside and opened it against the ache.

This one didn't lecture. It *built.* Where Baruffio had talked of stillness as a mood, Vavasour gave the method like a mason's plan: you make a *room* — an actual constructed space behind the eyes — and you decide what is kept in it and what is left out on the step for any passing mind to read, calm and ordinary and dull. Defence wasn't a wall. It was *housekeeping.* The first drill was plain: build the room nightly, set your surface thoughts in order on the threshold, and learn to hold the door against a little pressure without the strain showing.

He tried it. Not the shove from before — the *ordering.* Set the day's wreckage down, square by square, and quieted his surface. And the thread of *her* dimmed — not slammed off, but eased lower, cleaner, costing him less than the brute force had. It didn't hold; the moment his focus frayed it crept back. But it was the first time it had answered the *trained* way, and the difference was the whole point.

Later, the lodestone book — older, gentler reading. It spoke of kindred stones: a lodestone that finds north, and the elder lore beneath it, of *twinned* stones that lean toward one another across a distance, a stone that remembers the rock it was split from and aches, faintly, back toward it. Sympathy and kinship as the root of all finding. It put a frame around what the birch pair did — two things of one source, drawn home. It said nothing of horns, or of why a living creature's blessing should turn a wand into a needle. That part wasn't on any page.

The candle burned low. The room was quiet, and the books had given what they had to give.

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 20:55**

He went back into Ashworth properly, past the doctrine and into the chapters where she actually showed her working — *On the Settings of Common Virtues*, with her own diagrams in the margins.

Her rule had three parts, she wrote, and a man who forgot any one of them got nothing. A **virtue** — the stuff whose property you wanted. A **binder** — what packed and held it. A **casing** — what sheathed and concentrated it. *And all three must answer the virtue's own nature, or you have built it a prison instead of a horn.* She set her correspondences out by kingdom: stone and metal kept their own company; growing things wanted growing things; water wanted a vessel that held without quarrelling.

Then the worked forms, plain enough to copy.

**Stone — a disc.** Take rose quartz, she said: grind or cut it to a thin round, and ring it in a band of *silver*, for silver refines a mineral virtue and never fights it. The silver bezel was both the binder that held the disc and the shell that gathered its strength inward. He set one of the cloudy quartz lumps from his pocket on the open page, next to her drawing, and kept reading.

**Herb — a pellet, or a bead.** A dried herb would never hold alone; you ground it fine and worked it into a pellet with warm *resin* or *beeswax* — kin to the green thing, both once-alive — then sleeved it in a curl of thin wood, or sealed it whole inside a bead of hardened resin, like a fly in amber. Cold stone or metal round a herb, she warned, only deadened it.

**Liquid — a sealed vial.** You did not pack a liquid; you *kept* it. A small glass vial, or a capsule of horn, filled to the very brim so no air was left to let the virtue scatter, then stoppered and sealed under wax. Glass and horn held water's nature; a reactive metal soured it.

And running under all of it, her warning about the wrong shell — which he didn't need her to prove, because he'd had it proven on his own fingers an hour ago. *A casing of opposed nature does not merely fail; it turns the virtue away.* Dragonhide, made to turn a spell, had strangled one cold. Plain leather had simply sat there, neither helping nor hurting. Only a shell that *answered* would gather a thing up and aim it.

He turned to a clean page and started writing it all out in his own hand.

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 21:40**

The Muffliato settled over him like cotton wool packed against the ears — his own footsteps gone soft, the world held at arm's length. The greenhouse was dark and humid; the fouled merdrake glass vanished clean at a flick of *Evanesco*, and a stoppered demijohn shrank to the size of a thumb and went into his pocket. Athena came to his shoulder out of the dark when he called, a warm grey weight, her head already swivelling.

Hagrid's windows glowed gold across the pumpkins. He gave them a wide berth, down the slope, the wet grass dragging at his shoes, until the tree-line rose black ahead and swallowed the lamplight behind him. *Lumos.*

The forest took the light and kept most of it. Trunks crowded close, the canopy shut out the sky, and the dark past his little white glow was total and listening. Something moved, far off — a snapped branch, a long way down — and Athena's talons tightened on his shoulder. He worked inward and downhill the way Hagrid's mutterings had pointed, until the close pines gave way to older trees, and there they were: yews, black and low and spreading, and beneath them the ground was not level. Mounds. A dozen of them, more — some sunk and grassed-over with years, some raw and recent, each with its rough little marker: a set stone, a whittled stick, a knot of feathers gone to rot. A gamekeeper's graveyard, kept quiet under the death-trees.

Athena would not leave his shoulder.

## User

*walk and look around*

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 21:40**

He walked the rows slow, the Lumos throwing long shadows that swung with his hand. Up close the graveyard told its own quiet history. The oldest mounds had sunk almost flat, grassed and leaf-buried, their markers tipped and furred with moss. Newer ones rose clear of the ground, the earth still dark. Most were small — cat-sized, dog-sized, a clutch of tiny ones barely a handspan that he didn't let himself think too hard about. Hagrid had carved on some of the markers: rough letters gouged into wood, a name here, a date there, in a big clumsy hand that had pressed too hard.

Set a little apart, under the largest yew, was one long mound much bigger than the rest — old, settled, the length of something that had been *substantial.* And near the edge of the cluster, raw and recent, a fresh grave, the soil not yet sprouted, a pale new stake driven at its head.

His light caught something at an old half-collapsed mound off to the side — a dull gleam in the turned earth where some animal had been digging. Bone. Smooth and pale, a knuckle of it surfaced and weathered clean.

Above him, Athena shifted and gave a low, unhappy click, her eyes fixed off into the black downhill, not at the graves at all.

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 21:41**

He lifted the wand and turned the light down the slope, throwing it as far as it would go.

It didn't go far. The ground fell away into the trees and his little white glow died against the dark perhaps thirty feet on, and past that was nothing the light could touch — a wall of black that seemed to drink it. He held it there, straining to see.

And the dark gave something back. Right at the edge of the glow, where the light frayed to grey — eyes. A pale gleam, low, and another beside it, and another, steady and unblinking, catching his Lumos and throwing it back at him. Behind them the suggestion of shapes, *tall* ones, standing utterly still among the trunks. A hoof came down soft on the leaf-litter, a single deliberate sound. Something that might have been the wind moved through the dark, low, and might not have been the wind.

They weren't coming closer. They were only standing, several of them, watching him from the black at the bottom of the hill.

Athena's talons bit through his robe to the skin. She did not make a sound now at all.

He bent at the waist, slow and low, and held the bow — wand pointed at the ground, the light pooling at his feet, his eyes down.

Nothing, for a long moment. The forest held its breath with him.

Then a single set of hoofbeats, unhurried, coming up the slope — and when he straightened, one of them had walked to the very edge of his Lumos and stopped. The light found it: a broad chestnut body where a horse's would be, and rising from it the bare torso of a man, dark-bearded, a longbow held loose in one fist. Its eyes were what had caught the light — deep and unhuman and entirely unimpressed, fixed on him.

It looked at him for a while. When it spoke its voice was low and even, with no warmth and no haste in it.

"Few of your kind bow. Fewer mean it." A pause, the great frame shifting. "Yet here one stands, at night, among the buried dead, with a light in his hand. The forest is no place for a boy after dark — and this ground least of all." Its head tilted a fraction. "Speak, then, while I have the patience to hear it. What brings you down the hill?"

*I hand him the packet of golden hair.* "It was Tuesday afternoon, the thirteenth, just gone half two. A deep burnished gold-bronze, the whole of her. I know how that sounds: a grown one is meant to be white, gold's a foal's colour they lose inside a year or two, and adults don't let themselves be seen at all, least of all by a man my size. She broke every rule of that, and she broke all of them toward us.
I sat down in the leaves and made myself small, and called Athena, my companion owl right here, say hi Athena, to me, and the unicorn watched all of it — the wand I laid down, the little owl — and then she came. Of her own accord, right up to an arm's length. We both set our wands aside in the dirt. I talked to her the whole time, gave her our names, asked before I did anything. She let Mei and me stand and put our hands on her — the coat was impossibly soft, warm as sun though no sun reaches in there, and there was this slow, steady heartbeat under my palm. Athena hopped up onto her neck and gave her one nip, just inspecting her, and she bore it like she had all the patience in the world.
I'd nothing to offer her, no food, so I asked if I might groom her as a thanks, with Mei's bone comb, and keep only what came loose — nothing by force. She turned her flank to me and let me brush the whole of her. When I'd finished I gathered what had fallen and asked her properly, and she huffed once, then dipped her head and touched her horn to the back of my hand — take them — and walked back into the dark.
We coiled the hairs into an old ink bottle Mei had on her. It's mane, coat, and — the part I can still hardly credit — a whisker, a tactile hair off the muzzle. Not the tail, which is the only part anyone ever means by 'unicorn hair.' Mei says she's never read of gold hair off a grown one anywhere, nor of one being freely given to anybody. Neither of us has a name for what we saw, and I'd rather not invent one. It was, plainly, one of the finest afternoons of my life — and Mei was beside me for the whole of it."

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 21:47**

The centaur took the packet in two fingers as though it might wake. He tilted it to the Lumos, and the coiled hair caught the light and held a gleam that wasn't quite the light's — gold laid over gold. He was silent a long time.

"I have walked this forest since before your grandfather's grandfather drew breath," he said quietly. "I have never held this." His thumb found one finer strand among the rest. "And a whisker. The muzzle a unicorn yields to no creature living. She gave you the hair of it." He said it the way a man names something holy.

His eyes went up to the hidden sky and stayed there. "This is no chance, and it is not kindness only. It is a sign, boy — a thing *sent.* The heavens have been uneasy a long season now; your kind have troubled them" — the war, unspoken and plain — "and the old golden things are stirring that have not stirred in an age." Then, flat, final: "What it means, I will not say. The stars do not whisper their counsel to be repeated to a boy in the dark, and I will not name what I have not yet read clear."

He held the packet back out to him. "This is yours. She did not give it to the forest. She gave it to *you.*" The dark eyes weighed him, no longer merely wary — grave. "Whatever you are becoming, the forest has marked you. Tread it gently."

His gaze settled, steady. "But you asked the wrong question of the forest. The forest does not *make* a man what he is. It *uncovers* him. "What it is — that you will learn, or you will not. Not from me."

He turned his great body half away, back toward the slope and the waiting dark. "Now go back to your light and your walls, before the night asks more of you than questions. And do not dig here. The dead under these yews are kept. Leave them so."

That earned the ghost of something that wasn't quite a smile. Maelor unslung the bow and held it up into the light — a stave taller than Caleb stood, dark wood worn pale where the hand went, the string thick as a bootlace. He did not offer it across.

"A centaur's bow is not put in another's hand, and no man living could bend this one besides. Look, if you like. You'll not draw it." He let the Lumos travel the length of it, then slung it again. "But your feeling did not lie to you. Yew is the truest wood a bow was ever cut from. That you reached for it without being told is — not nothing."

He regarded Caleb a moment. "Come back rightly. By daylight, on your feet, asking and not taking. Do that, and it may be the cutting of a stave and the curing of it is a thing you are shown. The forest gives to those who ask it properly. It takes from those who do not."

He turned fully now toward the dark and the waiting herd. "And we are no owls, boy. We keep our own hours, and walk by night when the sky asks it of us." A last look back over the chestnut shoulder. "Go home, Caleb. You've had more of this forest in one night than most are granted in a life. Do not spend the luck all at once."

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 22:00**

Maelor huffed — a short, dry sound through the nose that in a man might nearly have been a laugh. "Perhaps I would have," he allowed. "Go on, before I am tempted to learn your bad habits." And then, almost grudging: "Be well, Caleb."

Caleb bowed deep and held it, and when he rose the centaurs were already folding back into the black, hoofbeats softening downhill until there was only the forest again.

He walked out the way he'd come, Lumos low. He kept his eyes off the long mound under the great yew — but it sat there at the edge of his light all the same, settled and patient and far too big, and he let it be. The smaller graves slid past in the dark.

The flora caught his eye where the slope leveled: pale night-things, a vine he didn't know with black glossy berries, and once — low in a damp seam between roots — that faint silver glint he knew well by now, a little knot of the night-flowers burning their cold light to no one. He marked where they were and left them rooted.

Then the trees thinned, the ground rose, and the Forbidden Forest let him out under an open sky scattered with stars. Hagrid's lamp was dark across the grounds. The castle stood high and gold-windowed above him, and the night air was clean and cold after the close dark behind.

"Muffliato" *go inside to ravenclaw boys to my bed and sleep* [[commit everything]]

## Narrator

▼ **Sunday, 18 September 1977 — 22:30**

"Muffliato." The buzz closed over him again, and the dark castle let him pass — up the moving stairs, along the empty seventh-floor corridor, the eagle-knocker drowsing, the spiral up into the tower.

The dormitory was warm and dim and full of the breathing of sleeping boys, the curtains drawn round their four-posters. He found his own, toed off his shoes, set the cherry wand on the nightstand and the rest in a heap he'd sort tomorrow, and lay down in his clothes with no will left to undress.

The day went out of him in pieces. Somewhere across the castle the bond carried its faint quiet thread, neither pressed nor walled, just there in the dark like a hand left open. He let the surface of himself go still the way the book had shown, more out of curiosity than need — and somewhere in the trying, the long Sunday finally let him go, and he slept.

*awaken at 7 and immediately focus on my surface thoughts. then sit there and try to do my mind house building*

## Narrator

Morning. New day — running the day's checks against the queues, then we open on the drill.

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 07:00**

He woke at seven, before the others stirred, the curtains still drawn round his bed and grey light leaking through the gap. He didn't get up. He lay still and went straight at the surface of his own mind the way the book said — the first thoughts of the day, the leftover weight of the last one, gathered and set in order on the threshold, plain and dull, nothing for anyone to catch.

Then he sat up cross-legged in the rumpled sheets and built.

It came easier than it had in the office. No raw grief thrashing under it tonight to fight against — only a rested quiet, and the slow ordered work of raising the room behind his eyes, wall by clumsy wall. It was a crude thing, lopsided, nothing like Vavasour's clean architecture. But it stood. For a span of breaths it actually *stood*, and under it that ever-present faint thread of the bond went smooth and low and far, quieted not by force but by the simple ordering of the house around it. No ache this time. No bleed. Just the room, holding — until his attention slipped a hair and a wall went soft and the whole of it sagged gently back into ordinary morning.

Better. A long way still to go, but better.

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 08:08**

Pince's quill went still. She took the list in as a single object, and her expression suggested she was revising her opinion of him in two directions at once.

"Centaurs. The disposal of corpses. The currency markets. And *arrow-making.*" Flat as slate. "Before nine. On a Monday."

She dealt with the corpses first, because they offended her. "There is no shelf for hiding bodies, Mr Waddell — effectively or otherwise. Were there, I should not hand it to a seventh-year wearing that particular expression. Ask again and you'll find the door."

The centaurs earned a fractional thaw. "Those we have. Third alcove, Beings and Beasts, lower shelf. But understand — nearly all of it was set down by wizards who watched centaurs the way one watches weather, and a centaur knows when he's being watched so. If you mean to walk into that forest and insult no one, take *Brackenridge.* She sat with a herd and was *permitted* to, which the others cannot say. The rest will teach you a centaur's height and nothing of his manners."

A brisk verdict on the last two. "Gold against Muggle money is Gringotts' business, and goblins do not publish — so we are nearly bare there. What little we hold is dry Ministry economics, stale the day it's shelved. Fletching is under practical crafts, what there is of it; we were never a people much given to arrows." The quill rose. "Now — which of those do you truly need before your lesson?"

## User

"we have 50 minutes yet before class, so I will work on centaur reading now."

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 08:38**

The green book had a smell to it — old paper, older patience. Brackenridge did not write like the others. Where the naturalists catalogued, she apologised; her opening pages were an account of three years spent at the treeline being ignored, until the herd decided she had not come to take anything.

A few things rose off the page that he'd got right by luck. *To a centaur there is no such thing as half. He is not half a man. To name him so is the oldest insult, and he will not forget it in your lifetime.* And, lower, on the very thing he'd watched Maelor handle: *Their archery is no sport. A bow is a thing a centaur makes once and keeps. To be shown its making is to be counted, for that hour, as kin.*

On the heavens she went carefully. They read the sky as men read weather — but in centuries, not days; they will name a sign and refuse, flatly, to tell you its meaning. *Not from secrecy,* she wrote, *but from scale. They think it impertinent to fix a meaning to a thing still moving.* And underlined, faint, by some earlier hand: *Never ask a centaur his name. If he gives it, he has given you something. Spend it carefully.*

Beside him Mei had given up the pretence of her own page and was reading his over his shoulder. Somewhere below, the half-hour bell came thin up the stairwell. A little time left yet.

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 08:50**

Inside, the long room had been stripped to its bones — desks stacked along the walls, the floor bare and waxed, a handful of seventh-years standing about in the cold light not yet properly awake. At the front Henshaw had his sleeves shoved to the elbow and was walking the last bench into the wall with his shoulder rather than his wand.

Caleb drew the birch. He didn't speak it — only held the shape of *Protego* in his head and pushed it down his arm.

It came clean. A faint curved shimmer bloomed a foot off his hand, the air going briefly thick and glassed, and through the open ribs the white hair showed pale, lit for a moment by the charm's own light. The birch ran warm. Steadier than he'd half-expected of a wand barely a day old — quieter than the cherry, somehow; less like throwing a wall up than like the wand had simply agreed to it.

Beside him Mei watched the shimmer hang and fade and said nothing, but the corner of her mouth gave — she'd built the twin of the thing in his hand, and it showed.

Near the front, Henshaw straightened and cast an eye over the filling room. Not quite nine.

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 08:53**

For a few minutes there was just the two of them and the small private weather of it — Mei's shield blooming a beat after his, then his after hers, two faint glassed curves rising and thinning in the cold light. Lips still, both of them; the charm going down the arm instead of off the tongue. The birch stayed warm and willing. And when their shields hung at once, a foot apart, the pale hairs in both wands caught the same light and held it — as though each were faintly aware the other had woken.

Neither of them spoke. There was nothing the casting wasn't already saying.

Then the hour. Henshaw crossed to the door and put it to with a flat thud and the click of the bolt — nine o'clock, and the last stragglers were on the wrong side of it. He turned back to the room, sleeves still shoved past his elbows, and the loose talk died without his having to ask.

"Wands out. No books — you've nowhere to set them down today in any case." His eye went once round the bare floor, the stacked desks. "We're doing shields. Properly. The kind that hold when something *means* it."

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 08:55**

Tip met tip with a small dry click of birch on birch. They cast together — both silent, both holding the same shape behind the teeth.

This time the two curves didn't rise side by side. They reached for one another and *joined*: the shimmer ran out from the touched points in one continuous sweep and closed over the pair of them, a single low dome of thick, glassed air. Brighter than either alone. Steadier — it didn't shiver and thin the way a held *Protego* always wants to. Inside the open ribs both white hairs had gone to a hard pale blaze, and the birch in his hand was warm now to the point of nearly humming — that same not-quite-sound he'd felt the evening the joined tips swung north toward the lean-to.

It held. And went on holding. Over the joined wands Mei's eyes came up to his, bright and a little startled — *that's new* written plain across them, no need to say it.

Across the room Henshaw had stopped mid-sentence. He was looking at the dome of light closed around the two of them with an expression gone very still and very interested — the face of a man quietly revising his lesson plan.

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 08:57**

Henshaw didn't make them ask twice. "Hold it, then." His wand came up unhurried — and the first thing he threw was no teacher's tap. A Knockback, solid, the kind that puts a fourth-year flat on the boards.

It broke on the dome. The jinx splashed wide in a spray of blue, the glassed air ringing like a struck bell, and inside the open ribs both white hairs flared hard under the blow. The shield didn't give an inch.

Something kindled behind Henshaw's eyes. The second came harder, and without warning — a lance of force that cracked against the curve like a hammer on ice.

That one they *felt.* It ran down the joined tips and split between them, a jolt up each arm at once — shared, halved — and the dome flexed and shimmered and held. Held like struck glass that has decided not to break.

Henshaw lowered his wand, slowly. The rest of the room had gone entirely quiet. His gaze moved from the two wands to the two of them and back.

"Right," he said, to no one and everyone. "That is *not* what two shields do."

This time he didn't reach for her, and he didn't reach for the curve of light. He reached for the thing underneath both — the plain, flat want to put something solid between the world and the two of them, and to hold it there.

It struck the dome and *stopped.* No splash. No ring. Swallowed, like a stone dropped into deep water.

Sage set them to weighing out antimony and disappeared into the stores, which left the long bench, the scales, and ten minutes of usable quiet. Caleb gave Liri both stories low-voiced over the work — the graveyard, the bow, Maelor, the gold portent; then the tea, the *"I'm the only one who's buried someone,"* the name that landed like a dropped bell, the morning's mending.

Liri weighed and listened and didn't interrupt once, which from her was a courtesy with teeth. The centaurs got her first.

"He gave you his *name.*" The scoop paused mid-air. "My mam-gu would've sat you down for that. In the old houses a name freely given is money — you don't spend it twice in a market." A beat. "And '*they do not appear, they are sent.*' Duw. We stood in front of that creature, Caleb." She let it sit, then filed it the Liri way: "Three o'clock. I'll want the whole of it after."

The tea she took quieter. "Mei's told me her mother's weather, some. I'll only say this — '*only I've buried someone*' isn't an argument anyone builds on the day. That was loaded years back. You just walked nearest the grave." Her eyes flicked toward the stores door; Sage's footsteps were coming back. "Glad you two sorted the rest of it. Now look like a man who's weighed his antimony."

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 10:56**

Sage came back with a tray of stoppered reagents, set it down without a clink, and regarded him over the half-moons in that way she had — as if he were a sample that had just announced its own purity.

"The scale weighs what's on the pan, Mr Waddell. It is famously indifferent to autobiography." She began setting out the jars in a precise line. "But since you raise it — metal carrying a great load wants annealing now and again. Worked hard and never softened, it cracks where you can't see." A glance, brief and not unkind, that took in the pair of them and whatever she'd half-heard from the stores. "I trust you anneal."

Then, briskly, the lesson resuming in her very next breath: "Antimony into the crucible, low heat. Today we watch what it does just *before* it changes — the part everyone misses because they're waiting for the interesting bit. Begin."

Liri slid him the tongs, mouth perfectly straight. "You heard her, Atlas."

"It is," Liri said of the Atlas, pleased despite herself. "Holding the whole head up and getting none of the credit. Very Welsh."

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 11:07**

Something rare crossed Sage's face — not a smile, exactly; the look of a sample testing pure.

"Correct." She let the word stand alone a moment, so it would count. "Most students watch a melt and see only the after. Everything interesting in my craft happens in the *deciding* — the straw before the blue, the bloom before the slump. Catch a substance while it's still deciding and you have a say in what it becomes. Once it's decided—" a small dry shrug "—you're only mopping."

She nodded at his parchment. "Write it as you said it. *Making a decision* will serve you better than half the Compendium's vocabulary."

Then, because she never let a good moment go unworked: "Now the other half. Off the heat with it. Watch it cool, and tell me whether it decides the same way going down as it did coming up. Miss Penrose — yours too. You have until the bell."

Liri pulled her crucible off the flame, murmuring, "Going down's always the harder decision," to no one in particular.

## User

Annwyl Mam-gu,

I should begin by confessing that I have stolen your address.

Not in a criminal way, I hope, though I admit that sounds exactly like what a criminal would say. Liri wrote it herself on an envelope, in her own hand, and I saw it, and I thought: well, that is a dangerous amount of power to leave lying around near someone with poor impulse control and very sincere intentions.

So here I am.

My name is Caleb Waddell. I am one of Liri’s classmates at the Scottish school — specifically, I am the other half of our entire NEWT Alchemy class, which is less impressive than it sounds because there are only two of us. It is me, Liri, and Professor Sage, which means Liri spends a great deal of time watching me try to look as though I understand things before she quietly does understand them.

I am writing because I wanted to tell you something true: you raised someone extraordinary.

I know that is the sort of sentence people put in letters when they want to sound grand, and I promise I am trying very hard not to be grand. I am not good at grand. I am good at animals, bad jokes, getting in trouble by accident, and occasionally making a thing better after I have made it worse. But I mean this plainly.

Liri is brilliant. Quietly, which is the dangerous kind. She has the face of someone who has been watching the room for six years, taking notes, and charging interest. She is dry, careful, funny when she decides you have earned it, and so sharp that if you handled her wrong you would come away missing fingers and deserve it.

For years, I am embarrassed to say, I knew her mostly as the girl who did not come to lunch.

That is on me. I should have noticed sooner.

This year I have had the luck of sitting beside her in Alchemy while we watched things refuse to happen for long stretches of time, which is apparently a serious academic discipline. We have watched lead calcine, worked with cold ethanol and Wiggenweld binding, waited on changes that take their own sweet time, and once, during a slow stretch, she agreed to teach me to braid hair. I do not know whether this says more about her patience or her misplaced faith in my hands.

She has also become part of Hexfield, the game I helped invent with Imogen Vale. Liri began as our announcer-in-training, with about twenty-four hours’ notice, and then very rudely turned out to be excellent at that too. She found the cadence, ran the light cues, and somehow managed to razz me in front of more than a hundred people without seeming to enjoy it, which I am told is one of her more advanced talents.

Then she decided to play.

She did not tell me at first because, in her words, I would have made a speech. She was right. I would have made a speech. It would have had feelings in it and probably a metaphor involving a wet owl. She spared us all.

And then she stepped onto the pitch and became lethal.

There is something very satisfying about watching a quiet person discover that the world has underestimated her and that she has no obligation to correct them gently. Liri started stinging people clean across the pitch without leaving her feet. She bantered in front of the school. She took up space. Not loudly. Not falsely. Just enough that people had to notice she had been there all along.

Professor Sage said something about her that I think belongs to you more than it belongs to me. She told me, after watching Liri come out into the light that day, that I was not making Liri into anything. Liri was already made. I was only one of the conditions under which the pattern came up to where it could be seen.

That is the truest thing.

Whatever I have done — if I have done anything good at all — it is only that. I have been some weather. Some light. A bit of noise in the room. But the person underneath was already there.

You made her.

Or helped make her. Or gave her the house where she could be made. Liri said your house raised her, and I believe it. I believe it every time she stands steady, every time she refuses to be cheap with her opinions, every time she knows exactly when not to speak, and every time she does speak and the room has to catch up.

She told me about the Welsh cakes, too. Firm-edged. I mention this partly because it seemed important, and partly because I am angling shamelessly for one someday.

There is another thing I wanted you to know.

Your Welsh did something beautiful.

I am not going to explain all of it, because some stories are not mine to send down the post, and some things at this school sound even madder when written by me. But Liri spoke Welsh to a very old Welsh wise-woman — old in the way stories are old — and the two of them fell into a conversation I could not follow. I mostly stood there being decorative and useless, which is a familiar role for me.

But I understood enough to know what I was seeing.

The words you kept alive in your house, out of stubbornness or love or both, reached across three centuries. Liri’s Welsh — your Welsh, through her Cardiff mouth — mattered. It made a bridge where nothing else would have.

She said, or near enough, that she would have to tell you there was now a three-hundred-year-old wise-woman in it, and that you had made her interesting to the neighbours.

For the record, I think she was already interesting to the neighbours. But this will not hurt her case.

I am also supposed to be careful here, because if I write something like “Liri is completely in love with me,” she may murder me with punctuation, and I would like to live. So I will say instead that I love her very much, in the cleanest and most grandmother-safe sense of that sentence, and also in the sense that I adore the person she is becoming brave enough to let people see.

She is dear to me.

She is dear to more people than she knows.

And if I have any useful place in her life, I hope it is not that I changed her. I hope it is that I noticed. That I made a bit of room. That I cheered too loudly, teased too much, and stood close enough that she could step forward without feeling watched like a test.

You raised a hidden gem, Mam-gu.

The school is beginning to notice.

I thought you deserved to know before the rest of us start taking credit.

Gyda chariad,

Caleb Waddell

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 11:14**

She looked at the envelope a long moment, the way you look at a door you're not sure you want opened. Then she broke the seal with her thumbnail and read it.

He watched her brace for embarrassment around the second paragraph — and then stop bracing. The bench noise went on without her. At *the girl who did not come to lunch* her jaw set, very slightly. She read steadily through the braiding and the pitch and Sage's pattern line, and her thumb pressed flat against the parchment edge at *You made her.*

It was the Welsh that did it. *The words you kept alive in your house reached across three centuries.* She read that part twice — he could see her eyes go back up — and when she came to *grandmother-safe sense* she made a small damp sound that wanted to be a laugh.

"Fumes," she said, to no one, blinking at the crucible.

She folded the letter back along its creases with both hands, precise as a charm, and set it down in front of him.

"Send it. Don't change a word." Her voice came out level on the second try. "And Caleb Waddell — if you ever tell a living soul my eyes did anything in this classroom, I will write your *mother.*" A breath; the Cardiff softened by half. "She'll keep that letter in the dresser drawer with the important things. You should know that's what you've done."

Sage, at her desk, turned a page with conspicuous attention.

He watched his metal with the intensity of a man auditing it.

It rewarded him, actually. Going down was a different decision entirely — slower, more reluctant; a skin forming first across the top, then the set creeping in from the edges toward the centre, the last bright liquid holding out in the middle like the final word of an argument.

Sage finished the letter, squared its creases, and held it back out without looking up from it.

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 12:15**

The hour ran the way good bench-hours do — two quills, one low fire, nothing needing to be said. He wrote his half-page and kept *held breath* and *the final word of an argument* in it, as instructed. Liri swept her notes together and shouldered her bag. "Right. Food. And you can post your burglary on the way down." She held the door with her hip again — apparently a personal architecture by now. "After you, Atlas."

## User

"they like plants... probably.." *I read a little bit of the fletching book to get an idea what the book is about*

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 13:05**

"Probably," Mei agreed. "Everyone likes plants until Sprout hands them the gloves."

He fished Pellingham back out of her bag and skimmed it over the last of his plate. The scuffed old thing turned out to be a working manual, not a sporting one — written for keepers and wardens, plain as a fence post. Pellingham held that an arrow has four arguments: the *shaft* (spine matched to the bow's weight, grain dead straight "or the arrow lies"); the *point* (purpose); the *nock* (cut true to the grain or the shaft splits at the worst moment); and the *flights* — which were the actual fletching, the feather-work the whole trade was named for. Three feathers, one cock against two hens, all cut from the *same wing* so they curve together and spin the shaft. The spin was the secret. The spin made it fly true.

And the preface carried the old man's whole creed in one line: *a crooked shaft well-fletched flies truer than a straight shaft badly feathered — the feathers correct what the wood confesses.*

Everything in it was once-living, he noticed. Wood, feather, sinew, hide glue. Pellingham never said why. Pellingham didn't seem like a man who asked why.

Liri stood, brushing crumbs. "Bell in ten. Greenhouses, you two."

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 13:15**

The walk down was September doing its work — grey light, wet grass, the lake colourless as pewter. Mei peeled off for her own class at the Entrance Hall with a *three o'clock, lawn door* and Liri with a wave that managed to be both fond and a warning about centaurs.

Greenhouse Four was warm and smelled of cut green and damp loam. Sprout was already moving down the benches laying out secateurs and trays — autumn cuttings, by the look of it, the hedging stock to be taken before first frost.

She caught him at the door, earth on her cuffs, her usual brisk warmth — and one pointed addendum.

Then she was past him, clapping the room to order. "Right, you lot! Hardwood cuttings — taken right, a cutting is a plant that remembers being whole. Taken wrong, it's a stick. Today we learn the difference. Benches!"

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 14:52**

The cuttings went well — clean cuts below the node, lower leaves stripped, firmed into gritty compost — and Sprout passed his tray with a nod. The bell let the others out at quarter to.

At the merdrake bench she looked over his three glasses with her arms floured in compost to the elbow.

At his nod, she made the satisfied sound she kept for tidy method. "Good. That's the whole point of it — light on its own, not light *and* everything light feeds. Keep that discipline up or the comparison's worthless."

She nodded along the row — the tarped one, then the lit glass left furred with green. "And you're letting this middle one keep its algae, to see if the plant minds. Sensible. Log what you see in *both* — whether the furred one's mandrake lags the clean one, or doesn't care a jot. That's your actual question, and it lives in the difference between these two." She straightened, brushing her hands. "Dark-check's after seven, as ever. Off you go — you've that look. Mind the forest's wet underfoot."

## User

*read about bow string and rosin*

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 14:55**

He read as he walked up the slope, Pellingham open against one hand, the lake greying off to his left.

The string, it turned out, was its own craft. Laid up from flax or hemp — sinew or gut for hunting bows — and never one thick cord but many fine waxed strands spun together, because, Pellingham wrote, *a string is a rope of small agreements:* no single strand carries the draw, they share it, and a frayed one is cut out and replaced before it can part, never trusted on. Loops served at each end to seat the nocks; a centre serving where the arrow rides and the fingers pull, to spare the string from its own use. Length mattered — too long and the bow went sluggish, too short and it strained the limbs to breaking.

And the wax: beeswax cut with a little pine pitch, worked through the whole string to bind the strands and shed damp, *for a wet string is a dead string.* Rosin proper — the boiled resin, the fiddler's block — went on the *fingers* and the servings for grip, never the working length; rosin made things stick, and a string wanted a clean slip. The margin carried the old man's verdict: *a bow is only as honest as its string; mind the string and the bow will mind you.*

Same-wing feathers that agree. Many waxed strands that share one pull. He was beginning to see the shape of what Pellingham believed, even where the old man never said it.

Ahead, the lawn door, and Mei already there against the stone — books under her arm, the dark wood waiting beyond her.

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 15:00**

Hagrid's hut had smoke going and Fang baying behind the door before they'd reached it. He came out wiping his hands on a sack, delighted. "Caleb! An' Mei — come in, kettle's nearly—"

"Hagrid." Caleb asked it straight: the great long grave under the big yew, deep in. What's buried there?

The delight went out of Hagrid's face like a lamp turned down. His eyes cut past them to the dark line of the forest, and his huge hands went still at his sides. "...Now where'd you be seein' *that.*" Quiet, for him — careful, with something underneath that might have been old grief. "Some o' what's up there, I dug meself. That one—" a slow breath "—that one I didn'." He shook his head, once. "It's not a thing I'll talk about on a Monday wi' the kettle on. Some graves in that forest are best left sleepin', Caleb. Leave that one be."

He surfaced from it with visible effort when Caleb said where they were headed — in, by daylight, to meet the centaurs. The eyebrows climbed. "They asked yeh back? By *day?* They don' do that. Years I've been at that forest an' they'll barely give me the time." Wonder, and a thread of worry. "One gave yeh his *name,* yeh said. You mind yerselves in there. Bow to who bows first, an' don' go where they don' lead yeh."

The axe and saw he fetched without argument once Caleb said what for — a felling axe near as tall as Mei and a bright-worn bowsaw. "Yew's hard, an' it won' forgive a bad cut. If they're showin' yeh, do it to the letter." He pressed them over. "An' bring 'em back wi' all yer fingers."

Off to the side, under her lean-to, White Lightning had lifted her head at the sound of his voice.

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 15:09**

She was down in the straw where she always was — but her head came up off the bedding at his voice, ears forward, that much hers to give now.

He knelt in beside her. The silver flowers went off his flat palm a few at a time, and she lipped them up with that improbable gentleness, then let the long bone of her head rest against his chest while she chewed, heavy and trusting, her breath slow and warm through his shirt. He worked his fingers down her mane. A couple of minutes of nothing but that — her weight leaned into him at ground level, Mei crouched at the white shoulder with one hand spread flat, saying nothing and meaning everything by it.

Then a low whistle, and Athena dropped out of the grey in a slope of wings to his shoulder, talons finding their purchase, head swivelling once toward the trees.

Hagrid watched them to the edge of his vegetable patch and no further. "Mind what I said. *All* yer fingers."

They went in by daylight — axe and saw on their shoulders, books in Mei's bag, the owl riding the wind off Caleb's collar. The forest by day was a different country than the night one: green-grey light in long shafts, birdsong somewhere, the path soft and damp. But the quiet listened the same way, and the dark still pooled thick between the great trunks. Ahead, the land fell away toward the grove and its yews.

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 15:18**

The grove opened the way it had at night, except the dark had been pulled back off it: the low mounds under the yews lay plain in the green daylight, old ones sunk and grassed, the raw fresh one, and the great long grave humped beneath the largest yew of all. He kept his eyes off that one, mostly, the way you keep your eyes off a thing you've been told to leave.

He went to where the silver glint had been — and found them. Low in the leaf-litter at the very foot of the great yew's grave, rooted: the silver night-flowers. *His* night-flowers, the seep-blooms, the same waxy beaded kind he studies and feeds her.

But there was no shine. By daylight they were only small pale plants with dew that beaded and clung — the silver light that had pulled his eye across the dark was simply not there to see. He crouched and looked, and they told him nothing new. They only grew, here, of all the places in the wood — at the foot of the grave Hagrid didn't dig.

Mei had gone very still beside him. She'd seen where they were rooted too.

And the grove had that quality again — the listening quiet, the sense of having been noticed. Somewhere down the slope, under the trees, something was aware they had come.

The three of them bowed — Caleb deep and slow, Mei beside him with the etiquette she'd read into herself at breakfast, Athena dipping off his shoulder in a low pass and resettling. They held it.

For a moment the grove only listened. Then, downslope, the soft heavy fall of hooves on leaf-mould — unhurried, deliberate, a sound that meant to be heard coming.

Maelor came up through the green light between the trunks, the longbow at his back, and behind him at the treeline two more held still in the half-shadow, watching. He stopped at a courteous distance and inclined his head: the bow made once, and kept, and returned.

"You came back by day." Grave, and level. "And you brought the one you spoke of." His gaze moved to Mei, unhurried, weighing, and came back. Then it fell — to the felling axe, to the bright saw on Caleb's shoulder — and rested there a moment longer than was comfortable.

"And you came carrying iron to cut with." Not an accusation. A thing observed and set plainly down between them. "The wood has not yet said that you may. Tell me what you mean to do with it."

Maelor took the wand by the handle as it was offered, and held it up into the green light. A centaur with a wand in his hand — a foreign thing — but his face changed as he turned it and read the pale strand laid bare along the open ribs.

"You carry a true thing that none of your own people will believe, and you ask what is to be *done* with it." A slow breath through wide nostrils. "Not all knowledge is for doing. Some is only for keeping. We read the heavens in centuries and act on almost none of it. You were permitted to see truly — that is the whole of what was asked of you. He faced forward. "What it is, I will not pretend to say. That it is *real* — that, I will stand on."

Ahead, the trees began to thin around something older and darker-needled than the rest.

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 15:40**

Maelor inclined his head. "She is welcome to learn, if she chooses it — by her own will, not yours. We do not press a willing mind, and we do not refuse one." His gaze went to Mei and left the matter with her.

Mei set her bag down against a root. "I'll watch, and learn the craft of it. But the choosing of this one's his — it's what he woke up wanting." Arms folded, settling in. Her own call, plainly made.

"Good," Maelor said, of all of it. Then, to Caleb: "Your way is not foolish. A maker who comes by feel and takes his time spoils less wood than one who comes by rule and hurries. But feel alone leaves you standing at a tree till nightfall. I will give you eyes to go with your hands."

Caleb already had them on the trunk — both palms flat to the cold furrowed bark, circling, the deep red-brown grooves climbing up out of reach. Under his hands: damp, the chill of old wood, the great slow swell where root became trunk, the grain just readable beneath the surface. He reached for it the way the bond reaches — outward, attentive — and met mostly the plain, enormous fact of the tree. Old. Rooted. Wholly indifferent to him. If anything answered, it was too faint to name, and he would not pretend that it had.

"You feel the grain." Maelor was close now, vast at his shoulder. "Follow it up with your palm. Runs it straight as falling rain, the stave throws true. Twists it — and yews love to twist — the bow fights you all its life. Find me the straightest face of this tree." A pause. "Then we will speak of whether it can spare what you would take."

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 15:50**

He followed the grain with his palm, slow, the way Maelor told him, and asked when he wasn't sure — and Maelor answered every time without once doing it for him.

Three faces of the trunk twisted under his hands, the grain spiralling off like a wrung cloth — *yew's habit,* Maelor said, *it would rather corkscrew than grow honest.* But the fourth, the north-east face turned away from the prevailing weather, ran nearly true: the grooves climbing straight as falling rain for a clean span above the root-swell, no knot, no burl, no check where a stave would lie. He found it as much by the change under his fingers as by anything — the place the wood stopped fighting him.

"That is your face," Maelor said. He set Caleb's knuckle to the bark and had him knock — the live, firm answer of sound wood, not the dull note of rot. He named the rest: the pale back and the dark belly a yew carries together, one to resist the stretch, one the crush — "why this wood above all is the bowyer's prize; it is two woods grown as one, already half a bow before you touch it."

Then, before any iron: "Ask it. Not for show. This tree is old and keeps no one and has wood to spare — but you take rightly, from a thing that can give, or you do not take at all."

Caleb laid his hand flat to the chosen face and told the yew, plainly, what he meant to take and why.

The wood was warm now under his palm — only from his own hand on it, surely. Maelor unslung nothing, said nothing, and waited to see whether he would do the next part right.

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 15:56**

He set the pot of grafting tar on the leaf-mould where he could reach it, lid off, ready to close the wound the moment it was made.

Then he put his ear to the chosen face and waited. He let his own breathing go long and even, let the heat of the walk bleed out of him until he could no longer feel the line between his cheek and the cold bark — so that whatever the tree was, he would know it next time by the change. He waited until the small busyness of the wood went still around them: a beetle somewhere ceasing, the high birds settling, even the two at the treeline gone statue-quiet. And into that quiet he asked — to take only enough for a stave and no more, never enough to kill it; and asked it to show him what he might do for it in return.

The wood gave him the ordinary things. The deep cold of it. The faint living creak as the canopy shifted far overhead. His own pulse, slowing, loud in his own ear. If there was warmth, it was the warmth his cheek had left there. Nothing answered that he would swear to.

Maelor had gone very still, watching him the way you watch a thing you did not expect to see done. "It will not speak to you," he said at last, quiet. "Wood does not. But you asked, and you meant it, and you brought the means to close the wound before you opened it. That is the whole of the answer to your question — *what can you do in return.* You take little. You seal it. You do not come back greedy. You let it stand and heal. And you make the bow *well,* so the gift is not thrown away." A beat. "You listen to a tree the way you listen to the white one. I do not know what that makes you. I know it is not nothing."

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 16:05**

"Not the axe." Maelor said it before Caleb's hand could reach for it. "The axe is for felling, and you are not felling. The saw. And never a ring around the trunk — girdle it and you have killed it for one bow. We take from the one face, and the tree lives."

He talked Caleb through every stroke. Two clean cross-cuts, top and bottom of the stave-length, down through bark and pale sapwood and a measured bite into the dark heart — "with the grain, not across your fear; let the teeth do the work, you only steer them." Then the lever, and the split: the billet came away down the straight north-east grain the way Maelor said it would, yew giving true where it was asked truly, the pale back and a wedge of dark belly together — two woods in one, already half a bow.

He took that much, and no more. The cambium stood unbroken around the rest of the trunk.

Then the tar, worked thick into the open wound, edge to edge, sealed against the rot and the wet and the boring things — the return promised, paid in deed, before he ever walked away with the gift.

Maelor looked at the closed wound, then at the green stave in Caleb's hands. "Done as it should be done." Quiet, and it carried weight from him. "Now the hard part, which is the waiting. That wood is alive and full of water and will fight you if you hurry it. I will tell you how to season it — slow, and cool, and patient, over more moons than you will like."

## User

"yes, I will wait to do it correctly. I will follow what you instruct me to do"

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 16:06**

"Good." Maelor said it like a door closing on something settled. "A maker who will wait is already most of a maker."

He told it plainly, and Caleb listened. The bark stays *on* the back for now — strip it green and the back checks and the bow is dead before it lives. The ends get sealed, same as the wound — tar, or wax, so the wood loses its water slow and even and not in a rush that splits it end to end. Then it lies somewhere cool and dry and dark and *out of the wind* — never by a fire, never in the sun — up off the ground, turned now and again so it seasons true and doesn't warp to one side. And it waits. "A year is honest," Maelor said. "More is better. You will want it sooner. Wanting it sooner is how staves are wasted."

He looked at the green billet in Caleb's hands, and then at the sealed scar on the tree, pale tar against dark bark.

"Bring it back to me when it has given up its water — next spring, the spring after — and I will show you the next thing: the tillering, where you teach the wood to bend without breaking its faith. That is a long teaching, and not for today." The light had gone amber and low through the trees. "You have done a full thing today. Few of your kind would have done it so."

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 16:08**

"Not a branch." Maelor almost smiled at the thought. "In the open air it would swing in the wind, swell with every rain, dry in every sun, and check to ruin by midwinter. *Out of the wind* I said, and a branch is nothing but wind."

He considered the rest properly, the way he considered everything — and Caleb could see him weighing more than the wood. "The forest *can* season it. There are dry places — under the close evergreens where no rain reaches the ground, a dry hollow, laid up off the earth on dead-fall, turned when you come. Cool, still, dark. It is the right kind of place." A pause. "But hear the whole of it before you choose. This is not your wood. If your stave rests here, it rests under the herd's sufferance, not as a thing of yours planted in our ground — and I cannot promise it. The forest is not a safe place to leave a thing you love. Other creatures pass where I do not. I will tell the herd it is there and not to trouble it, and that is the most I can give you — which is not the same as safe."

He let that sit. "A cold dry corner you keep yourself, behind your own walls, is the *surer* keeping. The wood here is the truer keeping — it would season where it grew, and you would have to come back to it, again and again, to turn it." His dark eyes held Caleb's. "One is safe. One means something. I will not choose that for you."

Before he let himself be led anywhere, Caleb went back to the tree once more.

"One more thing — a kinder one." He laid his hand to a low, supple branch, well clear of the sealed stave-wound, and told the yew plainly: that he meant to take a small cutting, not to use it, but to *grow it on* — to put a piece of it down somewhere else where it could become its own tree, so the taking of the stave was paid back not only in healing but in another yew, somewhere, one day. Then he took the scion clean — a short green branch-tip, a graft's worth — and sealed the little wound with the last of the tar.

The mare watched him do it, and the dryness in her face had gone quiet. "He takes a bow from it," she said to Maelor, low, "and leaves it a child." She didn't explain the remark. She didn't have to.

Maelor only said, "Now I am certain of you." Whatever that meant, he let it stand, and turned for the slope.

Then they walked him out — the three of the old people, the man, his mate, the owl — through the green-gold dimming light to the thinning of the trees, where the wood let go and the lawns ran up grey toward the castle. There Maelor stopped.

"Here the path is yours. Mind the stave. Grow the child well." A grave dip of his head. "Come back slow, wood-taker. You know the way now."

## User

"you all very clearly understand the woods and the workings of them so much better. I was just learning today about grafting. but perhaps you could tell me what kind of area this graft would do well in before we leave?"

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 16:20**

Maelor paused at the edge of the trees, and gave the question the same weight he'd given the wood.

"A yew is patient and it is slow, and a young one wants shelter, not glory. Do not put it in the open sun on its first day in the world — give it dappled shade, the way it would grow here, under bigger trees but not crowded by their roots." He looked out over the lawns as if measuring them and finding them wanting. "It wants ground that drinks but does not hold — never a wet hollow, never standing water; a yew will sit a thousand years in the right earth and rot in two in the wrong. A little chalk in the soil it likes. Out of the hard wind."

The mare added, dry: "And far from anything that grazes. The yew is gentle to almost nothing — leaf and seed will kill a beast that eats them. Plant it where the hungry and the foolish do not wander." A glance that took in Caleb, not unkindly. "You keep creatures. Mind that."

"Plant it as a thing meant to outlive you," Maelor finished. "Shelter it while it is young, as you would anything young. Do not drown it, do not bake it, and then leave it be. It will know what to do far longer than you will be there to watch." A last grave dip. "Now go. The light is nearly gone, and you have a stave to hide and a child to root. Slow, wood-taker. Always slow."

"Thank you all. Good day to you." A small bow, and then the wood released them — Mei's hand in his, the owl shifting on his shoulder as he leaned his head against her feathers and she clicked her beak in what passed, for Athena, as readiness.

The lawns ran up grey-gold ahead, the lake catching the last low light. Behind them, when he glanced back, the three centaurs were already gone, folded into the trees as if they'd never come up to the edge at all.

He walked the grounds with two errands in his eye now. For the graft: he read the land the way Maelor had taught him to read the tree — looking for dappled shelter, ground that sloped enough to drain, nothing boggy, nothing in the full teeth of the lake wind. A few places along the wood's margin and the lee of the greenhouses looked honest enough to hold the thought.

For the hive: he scanned the bramble and the older trees on the forest fringe, listening for the particular drowsy hum of bees still working the last of the autumn flowers before the cold shut them in — the wax he'd want for a bowstring one day, the way Pellingham had it, beeswax and a little pitch.

Mei watched him quartering the ground like a hound. "Two staves, a baby tree, and now you want a *beehive,*" she said. "Tell me what we're actually hunting and I'll have eyes for it too."

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 16:40**

"The wax seals the ends of this," he said, lifting the green stave, "so it gives up its water slow and even and doesn't split end to end while it cures. Maelor's whole sermon about *patience.* And the rest goes by for string, one day — waxed strands, like Pellingham says. Plus a home for the little one." He nodded at the scion. "Three things. Wax, a string's worth, and a hole in the ground."

Mei fell into the search beside him, and between the two of them the grounds gave up an answer faster. It was Mei who found the site — a pocket of ground in the lee of Greenhouse Three, sheltered from the lake wind, dappled under an old hawthorn, the slope just enough to carry the water off. "There," she said. "Drains, shaded, out of the wind, and nothing grazes between the glass and the wall. Your mare would approve."

The bees were a harder ask. The autumn light was thinning and the cold had most of them home already — but along the bramble at the forest's edge he caught it: a low, drowsy hum threading out of a split in an old dead oak, a few late foragers going in heavy-legged. A wild colony, tucked deep in the trunk. Not a thing to rob at dusk with bare hands — but found.

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 16:55**

He knelt in the lee of the greenhouse and dug with his hands. The earth there was good — dark and loose under the leaf-fall, cool, crumbling open without a fight, the hawthorn keeping the worst of the wind off his back. He worked a hole deeper than the little scion was long, broke up the bottom so the roots it didn't have yet would have somewhere soft to go, and settled the cutting in upright, firming the soil around it the way he'd seen Sprout do it that very morning — gently, no air left at the root, but not packed to stone.

When he sat back, dirt to the wrists, there was just a green twig standing six inches out of the ground against the grey wall, looking like nothing at all.

"That's it," Mei said, crouched beside him, and there was no mockery in it. "That's the whole of it. A stick in the mud that might be a thousand-year tree." She looked at it a moment longer. "It'll want watering in. And it might not take, you know that. Cuttings are stubborn." Then, gentler: "But Maelor said grow the child well, and you've started it right. The rest is just showing up."

The light was nearly gone off the lake. Somewhere up the slope a window or two had begun to glow.

He found her across the Hall — at the Slytherin table, of course, a little apart from her housemates the way Cass always sat, a book open beside her plate that she wasn't really reading. She felt his look land and glanced up, and one eyebrow climbed at the state of him from forty feet away.

So he did what he'd done before and didn't think twice about: crossed the Hall, Mei with him, and dropped onto the green-and-silver bench beside her like he belonged there. A couple of nearby Slytherins gave the Ravenclaw incursion a look; none of them said anything. They'd stopped bothering weeks ago.

"You look," Cass said, moving her bag to make room without being asked, "like you lost a fight with a greenhouse and the greenhouse felt bad about it afterward." A dry beat as she took in the hay, the dirt, the faint resinous reek of him. "Start with why you smell of unicorn and pine tar."

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 18:20**

The letter went to Flitwick first — Eldower's note, the apprenticeship, the trade-of-results. Flitwick read the seal as they walked and his eyebrows climbed. "*Garrick Eldower.* Of Slipshod Lane. You have been *busy,* Mr Waddell — we will speak of this properly, the three of us." He tucked it carefully into his robes.

The wands came out next, passed hand to hand in the dusk. Caleb walked them through it — the open ribs, the cores left showing on purpose, the freely-given hair; then the pair, and his own word for them. "Brother wands when they're a matched build. *Sister* wands when they're birch from the same tree — like ours. One tree, two wands." Flitwick turned Mei's over with a craftsman's reverence; McGonagall's sharp eye went straight to the join. "No metal in it at all," she observed. "Clever. Or mad. We shall see which."

Then the detour. White Lightning lifted her head as they came, and Caleb knelt and fed her the night-flowers — glowing now, silver in the failing light, little cold lamps going from his palm to her mouth. The three professors went quiet.

And then he drew the cherry, dipped a thread of silver water up out of the corked demijohn, and laid the wand-tip to the base of her spiral horn — and let go.

The silver did not run down. It *climbed.* It wound up the grooves of the horn against all gravity, a slow cold line of light sinking *into* the bone, a faint glow moving beneath the surface as the horn drank it up.

McGonagall made a small sound. She had reached, visibly, for the thing she always reached for — the explanation, the precedent, the name — and her face, in the glow off the horn, showed her finding nothing there at all.

"...Filius," she said quietly, not looking away. "*What* am I looking at."

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 18:23**

"Watch," Caleb said, and swapped the cherry for one of the birch wands — the open-ribbed one, White Lightning's own freely-given white hair laid bare along its spine.

Except — and it was so faint that Caleb wouldn't have sworn to it in front of anyone less than these three — the glow beneath the horn seemed to answer a shade *warmer* to the birch, the light moving up a touch more willingly, as though the hair in the wand and the horn it touched knew each other from somewhere.

Flitwick had gone up on his toes. "The wand does not *make* it," he breathed, comparing it against his memory of the cherry. "The water carries its own intent — but *that,*" a small clawed gesture at the warmer glow, "—that is the core answering the creature it came from. Or it is nothing. I cannot —" He stopped, helpless and delighted, a master of charms with no charm to name.

McGonagall had not blinked. "Three of us are standing in a wet field watching water walk uphill into a unicorn," she said, very level, "and not one of us can tell me why." She turned to Caleb. "Show me the rest. The well. *Now,* before I decide I imagined this."

Mei's hand in his, he led them in — past the lean-to, over the threshold of the trees, no distance at all, the dark closing cool and green around the five of them and the dusk almost gone.

And then the well.

He'd dug it and widened it himself, a low broad pool of it ringed in stone and root — and at this hour it had come alive. The night-flowers crowding its rim were *lit,* dozens of them, small cold silver lamps burning without heat in the dark, and the water they leaned over held the same faint sheen, a pool of dim moonlight with no moon above it. The glow threw the four faces around it pale and strange.

Flitwick stopped at the edge and said nothing at all, which from Flitwick was the loudest thing he could have done.

McGonagall came up beside him and stood very still, the silver light catching the sharp lines of her, her breath clouding once in the cold. She was a woman who had taught Transfiguration for decades, who had a name and a theory for very nearly everything that could be done with magic — and she looked at the burning flowers and the moonlit water and Caleb watched her arrive, the same place Flitwick had arrived, the same place he kept arriving himself: nowhere.

Behind them Henshaw had stopped at the treeline, his back to the marvel, watching the dark between the trunks. "Pretty," he said quietly, not turning. "Now tell them the rest, Waddell. While I keep the door."

The two jinxes left the wands and met in the air between them and *caught.* Not died — *caught,* like two hands seizing the same rope. The cores lit, both at once, a thread of pale light waking down each open rib where White Lightning's hair lay bare, and a taut held line strung itself wand-to-wand across the space between them, humming with the full weight of two Knockback Jinxes that had nowhere to go. The force was *there* — he could feel it shoving back up his arm — it simply would not be spent on its brother.

Caleb set his jaw and cast again, harder, throwing everything into it. The wand didn't gutter. It *held* — locked solid, refused the order, and threw the intent straight back down the line into his own hand, the recoil thumping warm against his palm. The line between the two wands pulled tighter and brighter and held.

Flitwick had both hands over his mouth. "They're *locked* — they''re throwing it *back* — that is not a misfire, that is a *refusal,* they will not —" He spun to McGonagall. "Minerva, the cores are *lit.*"

McGonagall stared at the strung line of light between the two children and the two wands cut from one tree. "*Where,*" she said, very quietly, "did you learn to make these."

## User

"we didn't. the design is my own. I took it and an original attempt to Eldower, and that is why he agreed to an apprenticeship. the rest is all woodworking principles."

## Narrator

▼ **Monday, 19 September 1977 — 18:37**

"We didn''t." He lowered the wand and the locked line let go all at once, the held force unspooling into nothing, the cores dimming back to bare hair along the ribs. "Nobody taught us this. The design is mine. I drew it, and I built a first one, and I took the design and that first attempt to Eldower —" a nod at the letter in Flitwick's robes "— and that''s why he offered the apprenticeship. The rest is just woodworking. Grain, joinery, no metal in the path. Principles."

McGonagall looked at him for a long moment.

"*Woodworking,*" she repeated, with the flat precision of a woman declining to let a thing be made small. "Mr Waddell, woodworking produces a chair. It does not produce two wands that lock arms and refuse a Knockback Jinx between them. Your *joinery* is sound, I don't doubt it — but the joinery is not what we just watched."

Flitwick was nodding rapidly, delighted and entirely unbothered by her point. "She''s right, she''s right — and Eldower *knew it,* that''s the thing, Minerva, Garrick Eldower does not take apprentices, he hasn''t in *years,* and he read this boy''s drawing and opened his door —" He beamed up at Caleb. "He saw what we''re seeing. You''ve made something the craft has no page for. *That* is why I have his letter in my pocket and not the wastebasket."
