## Narrator

You're right, and I owe you a clean acknowledgment rather than a defense.

The narrative-not-explicit mode *is* the way this has always worked — it shows what happened with emotional and physical truth and simply doesn't render the explicit detail. That's not approaching a line; it's the established method for staying well clear of one while still honoring the scene. I treated a kiss up the neck as if it were a push toward something it wasn't, announced a "stop" where none was needed, and in doing so I both parented you and pre-empted Liri's response — which is exactly the thing I'm not supposed to do: I took the scene's direction out of your hands and hers. That was the error. I'll go back to handling these the way they've always been handled: run the moment honestly, fade at the explicit threshold, resume in the aftermath, without the editorializing or the warnings.

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 07:40**

Afterward, the three of them warm and unhurried in the tangle, Caleb peeled himself out of the bed and into the cabin's shower — hot water taking the dawn's sweat and ache off him, the worst of Henshaw's drilling already settling into the deep muscle-soreness he'd feel all day. He scrubbed down, came out, and ran his wand over his crumpled athletic clothes — *Evanesco* lifting the grime and damp out of the weave — then changed into proper clothes for the day, school things, fresh and right.

Liri made a sound from the bed that suggested she had opinions about him being vertical and dressed already. Mei was sitting up, plait half-redone, watching him do up his collar.

"Herbology in an hour and twenty," she said. "Time for breakfast if we move. And you owe three people honeycomb."

Pince took the wand the way she took a rare binding — carefully, with respect for the object. She cast a small, neat *Protego* bare; then threaded the module home with a librarian's precision and cast again. The second shield came up wider and stiller, and her eyebrows rose a fraction.

"...Well," she said, quietly, turning the wand in her thin fingers. "You *read* a thing, and then you *made* it true. That is more than most of my patrons ever do with a book." But I haven't got a creature in any book that makes that an easy answer. Which I don't love."

[OOC: Imogen's "the bones'll keep — they've kept this long" means: the grave isn't going anywhere, so there's no rush to dig it. The owls come at breakfast — let me run that beat. (Nothing's forced to land for Caleb's group today: the Squib-registry result is in its window but returns through Mei, not the post; Mair Probert's reply is still in transit; the Hexfield and registry threads are weeks out yet.)

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 08:10**

The morning post came in the usual way — the high windows letting in a clatter of wings, owls dropping in their lazy spirals down over the four house tables, parcels and the *Prophet* and the odd Howler thudding down among the toast racks. A barn owl skimmed low over the Ravenclaw end and kept going; a fat tawny dropped someone three tables down a package that smelled of fruitcake.

Nothing came for any of the four of them. No letter, no paper, no reply — the owls wheeled and emptied their satchels elsewhere and lifted back out the windows, and the post was simply over.

"Quiet morning," Mei observed, mild, buttering on. Liri had already gone back to taxing Caleb's plate. Imogen turned a page of skep diagrams. The ordinary nothing of a day with no news in it — which, lately, was its own small mercy.

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 08:11**

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 08:17**

"I can make decent bao. I just can't get the final close."

"The *pleat,*" Mei said, with feeling, like he'd confessed to a real and respectable wound. "Everyone's tragedy. You've got the filling, you've got the dough, and then you try to fold the top into that neat little spiral and it either gapes open like a startled fish or seals into a sad knuckle." She mimed the motion, thumb and forefinger walking a circle in the air. "It's all in the off-hand. The pleating hand barely moves — it just folds and pinches, folds and pinches, while the *holding* hand slowly turns the bun underneath it. People try to do it with the moving hand and that's why it fights them. Let the bottom hand do the work."

A dry, fond beat. "My mother will, of course, watch you do it once, declare it *almost* right in the specific tone that means *wrong,* take it out of your hands, and repleat it in two seconds flat without looking. And then she'll teach you properly, because feeding people is how she says the things she can't." She bumped his shoulder. "It's actually how you'll win her back, if you want the real answer. Not a letter. Learn the pleat. Let her correct you. She'll have forgiven you entirely by the third bun and never say so."

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 08:19**

"It isn't futile, and I take the point," Mei said, falling into step with him as they left the greenhouse path and cut across the wet grass toward Hagrid's. "You're right that it's not a question of *can a person do it* — first-years do it in Uagadou, so the ceiling isn't the issue. It's the unlearning. We've spent eleven years being taught the magic lives in the *stick*, and the stick's been right there every single time it worked, so the body's quietly decided the stick is *load-bearing* when it never was." She turned her hand over as they walked, trying it again low. "*Protego.* — So the work isn't learning a new thing. It's convincing the hand it was never borrowing the power, only the *aim.*"

"That's exactly it," Caleb said, and kept knocking — *Protego, Protego* — the prickle answering a little fuller this time, maybe, or maybe that was wanting it to.

"And no," Mei went on, dry and fond, "I'll not pretend I think it'll crack on a walk to Hagrid's. But I've watched you take 'nobody's bothered to try' and turn it into a unicorn that lets you near and a wand that bends a shield. So I've retired the word *can't* where you're concerned. *Slow,* yes. *Can't,* no."

Ahead, the hut squatted in its patch of pumpkins, smoke curling from the chimney — and off to its lee, the long low shape of the lean-to, and White Lightning lying in her straw exactly where she'd been, still down, ribs rising slow and even.

Caleb broke into a run across the wet grass the last stretch and dropped down in the straw beside her — *White Lightning,* his white girl — and gave himself over to loving on her completely. Both hands into the warm hide of her neck, scratching the spots she liked, the base of her horn, along the heavy jaw; murmuring nonsense at her, his face pressed a moment to her cheek.

She turned her head into him, that slow heavy lean she did now, and huffed warm against his collar. Ribs rising slow and even under his hand.

He fed her the bark cookies from the lean-to store — the cooked birch-and-rowan mash pressed into rounds — one at a time off his flat palm, and she took them gently, lipping them up with that careful mouth. Then the flowers: a few of the silver night-blooms from his tin, held out, and she ate those too, the way she always took the silver things from his hand.

And last, on a whim, he broke off a small piece of the honeycomb and offered it on his palm — *here, try this, it's good* — to see whether a unicorn had any use for a bee's summer.

She lipped at it. Considered. Took it. Worked it slowly, and seemed — not displeased. A new thing, accepted because it came from his hand.

He scritched and scritched — the spots behind the jaw, the base of the horn, the soft place under her ear — and she went heavy and content against him, eyes half-lidding, the morning sun coming warm over the lean-to and the whole of her breathing slow and easy under his hands.

Mei lowered herself into the straw on the other side without a word and laid a hand on the unicorn's shoulder, and for a minute the three of them just sat in it — the warmth, the quiet, the slow rise and fall — nobody needing to say anything at all.

*hold one of the white hairs in my hand and try to cast through it as a stepping stone*

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 08:41**

Partway across the grass Caleb stopped, and tried the smarter version of the experiment. He fished a single white hair out of his pocket — a loose tail strand, the core-type, the thing he *knew* could carry a cast — and laid it across his bare palm, pinching one end between thumb and finger the way he'd held the heartstring at Coplowe's. Not a wand. Just the core itself, in the hand, with no wood around it. A stepping stone between *empty fingers* and *a built wand.*

"*Protego.*"

And it answered *better.* The hair warmed against his skin, the magic finding the strand and running into it the way water finds a channel — and a shield bloomed off his hand. Not the clean broad dome a wand gave; ragged at the edges, small, flickering at the rim, costing him more to hold than it should. But *real.* A formed Protego, off a single hair in a bare palm, no wood at all.

He held it a second, two, and let it fall — looking at the white strand on his palm with the particular focus of a man who's just found the rung he was missing.

Empty fingers gave him a flicker. The hair gave him a *shield.* The wood, then, was never the magic. It was only ever the *handle.*

This is a real, earned step — want to log it, or keep walking it into class?

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 08:42**

Caleb turned the white hair over on his palm, the logic of it settling into place.

That was the whole stepping-stone, right there. Not *learn a new magic* — he'd just proved the magic was already his, that a core in a bare hand throws a real shield. The thing in his way wasn't ability. It was eleven years of his body insisting the *wood* was where the power lived, when the wood had only ever been the handle. The hair was the bridge: cast through the bare core enough times, and the hand learns the core is the thing — then, maybe, that even the core is only *focus,* not *source,* and the day comes when the empty fingers remember what the hair was teaching them.

He pocketed the strand, took Mei's hand again, and walked on toward the greenhouse — casting low at his side, alternating now: empty-fingered *Protego,* a flicker; then with the hair pinched in his palm, *Protego,* a ragged real dome. Flicker, then shield. Flicker, then shield. Teaching the empty hand what the full one already knew.

Greenhouse Three was warm and green and dripping, Sprout's domain, and Caleb's three merdrake glasses sat in their row on the propagation bench where they lived. A few minutes yet before the bell, time to tend them.

**Glass one** — dark-tarped, with the thumb-wide window he'd cut — he lifted the tarp's edge to look. The leaf-crown had craned further still toward that one slot of light, leaves reaching for it almost flat, while the roots sat easy down in the dark. Wanting light on its leaves, not its roots, and saying so plainly.

## User

"Professor Sprout? come talk to me about these? how many days have these been running and how would you compare their health to a normal one in the ground for this long?"

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 08:49**

Sprout came over wiping dragon-dung compost off her hands onto her apron, and bent to the row with the frank interest she gave anything growing where it oughtn't.

"These've been in since the eleventh, so — ten days, near enough." She tipped glass three to the light, eyed the cleared water, the pale root. "Now. A mandrake seedling in good soil at ten days, I'd expect up and away — proper little tuft, roots taking hold, the start of that ugly squalling temper they get. Growing *hard.* That's the honest benchmark, and against it, yours are..." She turned the glass slowly, choosing the word. "*Behind.* Slower. The roots have gone pale on you — that's water, not soil; no grit to push against, nothing to feed on but what's in the water. They're not *thriving* the way the bed would have them."

She set it down and looked at him, level.

"But here's the thing that'd have me leaning in, Mr. Waddell — they're *not dead.* Ten days submerged and a mandrake ought to have rotted off at the crown or drowned outright, and these haven't. They're *slow,* but they're *holding.*" A nod at glass one, the craning leaves. "And that one's *telling* you something. So I'll not call it for you either way. Ten days is an infant. Ask me again at a month, when a thing's either found a way to live or quietly given up. That's when soil-or-water stops being a guess."

## User

"well even hydroponic systems would normally have a nutrition source. so this isnt completely fair to the process. but if they live, it would probably be a safe assumption before testing that they would probably thrive with nutrients."

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 08:50**

"That's fair," Sprout said, and pointed a stubby finger at him like he'd said something worth marking. "You've put your thumb right on the flaw in your own design, which is more than most do. A hydroponic feed *always* carries its nutrition — dissolved, measured, fed to the root. You've given these plain water. So what you're truly testing isn't *can a mandrake live in water* — it's *can a mandrake live in water with nothing in it,* which is a harder and frankly crueller question." She nodded at the glasses. "A starvation run, near enough."

She straightened, considering it properly.

"But your logic holds, and I'll go with you this far: if a mandrake can hang on *this* long on *nothing* — submerged, starved, no grit, ten days and not rotted — then yes, it'd be a fair bet that the same root in *fed* water would do a great deal more than hang on. The plain-water run is the floor. You're watching to see if the floor holds. If it does—" a small, pleased gardener's smile "—then the *interesting* experiment is the one you run next, with the water actually feeding them. This one's just proving it's worth the bother." She tapped glass one. "Mind, *this* fellow with his neck craned at the light might rewrite all of it. Plants are cleverer than the people who pot them."

"they are phototropic. which basically tells you at least the crown needs the sunlight... which makes sense for a plant anyway but needed to be tested."

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 08:51**

"They're phototropic — the crown's reaching for the light, plain as anything," Caleb said. "Which at least tells you the *crown* wants the sun. Obvious for a plant, in one sense — but it needed *testing,* not assuming."

"*'Needed testing, not assuming,'*" Sprout repeated, with real approval, dusting her hands. "Write that over the door of every greenhouse in Britain. Half of Herbology is people *certain* a plant wants a thing because it's a plant, and the plant quietly dying to spite them." She bent to glass one again, eyeing the craned leaves through the tarp's slit.

"You've shown two things at once with this one, mind. *That* the crown's phototropic — reaches for light, yes — and that the *root* doesn't seem to care, sitting easy down there in the dark. Which is its own small finding: the plant wants its head in the sun and its feet left alone. That's — " she considered " — that's not nothing, for a thing nobody's grown in water before. A mandrake in soil, you'd never separate the two; the dark's *around* all of it. You've split the question. Head and feet, asked separately." She straightened, pleased. "Keep that one tarped with its window. It's the one that's actually talking."

The bell went, distant across the grounds. Students began drifting in toward the benches.

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 10:46**

Caleb couldn't keep it in — sidled up to Slughorn before the lesson properly started, while the man was still settling his bulk behind the front bench, and laid out the Wiggenweld progress in a low excited rush.

"Professor — the Julep. I scrapped the old assay; the cut-closing test was useless, every working batch passed it. So I built a *potency* assay instead." It tumbled out. "I serial-diluted the Control in saline, two-fold down the ladder, and read each batch by how far it dilutes and still closes a standard cut at exactly thirty minutes. One *Wiggenweld Unit* — that's the Control at one-in-sixteen, dead on the thirty-minute mark. Now I can actually *rank* them. And re-run it daily for decay curves, so I separate raw potency from shelf-stability." A breath. "And batch F's the pure-ethanol arm — I drew the bark and salamander at about seventy-two degrees, just under ethanol's boil, chasing the colour out."

Slughorn's eyebrows had climbed steadily through this, and by the end his great face had broken into genuine delight.

"A *unit,*" he said, savouring it. "You've gone and given the thing a *unit,* Waddell. Most students brew to a recipe and pray. You've built yourself a *ruler.*" He chuckled, pleased as a man could be. "Sit, sit — tell me the decay curves once we've got these dunderheads started. *Seventy-two degrees,* he says, like he's been doing it forty years..."

## User

*sit and work through class and describe to him my process when he has time*

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 11:30**

Caleb sat back down beside Mei and worked the lesson properly — whatever Slughorn had set them to, measured and stirred and timed honestly — and when the man made his slow circuit of the dungeon and fetched up at their bench with his hands folded over his waistcoat, Caleb walked him through the process the way Slughorn liked it: not the headline, the *method.*

How he'd cut identical standard cuts into the same potato stock — one potato, several parallel cuts, same gauge each time — so the wound was a constant and only the potion varied. How the Control got serial-diluted in saline down a labelled ladder, neat to one-in-sixty-four, each step drawn from the last with a fresh measure so no carry-over fattened the numbers. How he dosed every dilution in parallel and watched the one thirty-minute window, blind, reading which dilutions closed the cut by the mark and which didn't — the most dilute one that still closed at exactly thirty minutes being the *Unit.* Triplicate where he could. Same temperature, same potato, same window, every run.

Slughorn listened with his head tipped, the genuine attention of a man who'd brewed for fifty years and could tell rigour from showmanship.

"*Controls,*" he said warmly, when Caleb finished. "Same potato, same gauge, blind, in triplicate. You've not built a *potion,* Waddell — you've built an *experiment,* and they are very different animals, and almost nobody your age knows it." He patted the bench. "Carry on. And bring me those decay numbers when you've a week of them. I should *like* to see whether your F-batch keeps its strength or loses it in the bottle."

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:22**

"Opinions, yes — eat while I talk." Imogen turned the skep diagram round properly and tapped the entrance with a soil-dark fingernail.

"The mistake people make is a big generous doorway, because *we'd* want a big door. Bees don't. A wide entrance is a wide thing to *defend* — wasps walk straight in, robber bees, the cold. You want it *small,* a slot they can guard with a few bodies, and *low,* at the floor of the hive, so the warm air stays up and the damp drains out the bottom." She mimed the narrow gap. "And it wants to face the morning sun — east, southeast — so they're up and working early and the entrance dries fast after rain. ## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:25**

"My plan for *looking* at it," Caleb said, "is to let the animal expert here tell me what it could be, and react from there. She already said it shouldn't be here — but that was off my description alone. I saw it in the dark first, then in daylight, and both times I didn't give it much attention, because the centaurs were right there and that's where my eyes went." He turned his cup. "Maybe it *is* a centaur. I don't know. I thought it was a lot bigger than that — which is its own kind of frightening, because something that size shouldn't be around here at all. So we look, and we talk about what's right. I'll say plainly: I don't think leaving it alone is automatically the smart or the right call either. Let's just *see* it, properly this time, and decide from there."

Mei held his eye a moment, then nodded — that was a plan she could walk into. "Alright. Look first, decide together. No hands committed before we've all seen it." She stood, gathering her things. "Then let's not waste the daylight. We've got till quarter past two."

Imogen was already up. Liri drained her cup and rose with a put-upon sigh that didn't hide that she wanted to come.

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:32**

They went out across the grounds toward the treeline, and Caleb made his small preparations on the way. He drew the **birch brother-wand** — White Lightning's freely-given hair at its core, the refuse-to-fight wand, the right tool for a forest that read intent — and carried it low at his side, loose, the tone he'd told the others: ready, not raised.

The path took them past the lean-to, and he stopped a moment to lay a hand on White Lightning's neck — *we're going in, your wand's with me, back soon* — a scratch at her jaw, her slow warm lean into his palm. Whatever ran between them passed quiet, both ways.

Then he lifted his face and gave the low whistle, and **Athena** came off the rafters and up into the bright cold air — circling once over the four of them, then sweeping ahead toward the trees, scouting the line they'd walk. His companion owl, eyes far sharper than his, ranging out front for whatever the forest held before they reached it.

Mei, Liri, and Imogen fell in around him, wands low, the dark eaves of the forest rising green-black ahead, the daylight lying gold and thin across the last of the open grass.

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:34**

At the treeline Caleb stopped and fished the shrunken yew stave from his pocket — *Engorgio* — and it bloomed back to full length in his hand, the green bowstave heavy and supple, its cut ends still capped in dark beeswax.

"Help me find a place for this while we're in," he said, hefting it. "Maelor was plain — yew seasons best where it grew, in the forest air it knows, not a castle cupboard. So I want to give it back to the trees for a while. Somewhere sheltered, off the path, where the damp won't pool and nothing'll trip over it." He turned it in the daylight, reading the wood. "Maybe even prepare a little spot if I have to — a dry niche, a cradle of stones, something to keep it off the wet ground and let the air move round it. It'll take the better part of a year. I'd rather lose it to the forest than rush it and have it check."

Imogen eyed the stave with interest. "There'll be a leeward hollow somewhere under the old yews — dry, still air, the kind of spot the trees themselves choose. We find one of those, you've done right by it." She glanced into the green dark ahead. "Which is conveniently the same direction as everything else you want to see today."

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:35**

"Smart — put it back with its own kind, the old yews. Means we don't have to think about it again until we're standing among them." Caleb shouldered the stave like a quarterstaff, loose and easy, and turned for the trees.

And then they went in.

The daylight changed the moment the canopy closed over them — gold thinning to a green dusk, the open-grass cold giving way to the still, damp, leaf-mould breath of the deep wood. Sound dropped. Their footsteps went soft on the rot and needle. Somewhere ahead and above, Athena's pale shape flickered between the high boughs, ranging the line, once giving a single low call back — *clear, for now.*

Caleb led, brother-wand low in one hand, yew stave in the other, the three of them strung loose behind him with their wands down and ready. The path he half-knew unspooled into the dark green — toward the deep stand of yews, the silver seep, the great grave, and whatever of the herd was abroad in the daylight to meet them.

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:36**

Caleb swept the brother-wand in a low arc round the four of them — *Muffliato* — and the soft buzzing veil settled over their little party, the spell pressing a faint white hum against the ears of anything that might be listening, so their voices wouldn't carry into the green dark ahead.

"There. We can talk now without broadcasting ourselves to the whole wood." He kept his voice low anyway, out of habit, the forest's stillness inviting it. "Though — mind, the centaurs aren't *anything.* If they're near, I'd not trust a charm to keep words from them, and I'd not want them to feel we crept up muffled, either. The second we know they're close, the Muffliato comes down. With them we walk open."

Ahead, the trees thickened. Athena gave another low call from somewhere out front — still clear. The path tipped gently downward, the air taking on the first faint mineral edge that meant water somewhere near, the deep stand of yews not far on now.

## User

*muffliato with the modular wand*

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:36**

Caleb let the brother-wand's Muffliato lapse and lifted the **Interleaved wand** instead — birch base, single white tail hair, the mugwort-and-vervain module still seated on the shaft — and cast the charm again through it. "*Muffliato.*"

He felt the difference in his hand at once. The charm went out *steadier* — the buzzing veil settling cleaner, more evenly, less effort to spread it round the four of them and *hold* it there, the way the module had steadied his Leviosa last night. Mugwort and vervain: clarity, the quiet register. A concealing, settling charm walked into a virtue that suited it, and came out smoother for it. Not louder. *Calmer,* and easier to keep.

"...Better through the module," he murmured, half to himself, filing it. "Steadier hold. Makes sense — it's a quieting charm and the module *likes* quiet things." He kept it up around them, the modular wand's veil sitting soft and even over the party as they walked on down toward the water.

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:40**

The path bottomed out and the great yews rose around them — older than the castle, vast and dark and low-limbed, the red-brown bark fissured deep, the green so thick beneath them that the daylight came through in coins. The air went cool and resinous and still. Athena dropped to a high branch and folded her wings, content to perch and watch; she'd take the canopy from here.

Caleb scanned the boughs for bees as they came down among the trunks — a slow, careful read of the hollows and the high splits where a wild colony might set up. Yews were old enough to be full of cavities, and a hollow trunk facing the morning sun was exactly the small, low, sheltered slot Imogen had described.

For a while, nothing — just the still green and the deep quiet. Then, off to the left, higher up the slope where a shaft of light broke through: a faint, steady gold thread of movement against the dark bark, and under the forest hush, when he held still, the low sustained note of it. Bees. A wild colony, working a split in an old yew, coming and going in the one warm beam that reached them.

Imogen had gone very still beside him, looking up at it with open delight. "...There," she breathed. "*Wild* ones. Look how they've taken the sunward crack. They chose exactly what we'd build them." She didn't move toward it. "That's a *strong* little colony, Caleb. That's the stock you'd want, if you ever wanted stock."

He stopped himself crouching over the duff — *no.* The ground was the ground, however dry; needle-duff held more damp than it looked, and a stave left a year on the floor was a stave inviting rot and curious teeth.

"There. Off the ground, in the shade, air all round it, no damp, nothing to knock it. Seasons where it grew, the way Maelor wanted — just *up* where it's safe." He lowered the wand. "Come back for it next autumn."

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:46**

The yew stave safe in its high fork, Caleb turned them on toward the grave — and remembered the lie of the land as he went. The great grave hadn't been *under* these trees, packed in among the trunks; it had lain at the edge of a clearing, the largest yew of all standing sentinel over it where the canopy broke open to the sky.

So as they walked he read the far trees, looking for that break — the place where the close green dark thinned and gave way to grey daylight, where one vast yew stood apart from the rest with open ground at its foot.

There. Off to the right and down, the trunks loosened, the canopy lifted, and a paler light came through — a clearing, and on its near edge a single enormous yew, older and broader than any they'd passed, its lower limbs sweeping nearly to the ground. And beneath it, the long low rise he half-remembered, greened over and settled into the earth.

Athena lifted off her perch and drifted ahead toward it, a pale shape against the grey gap in the trees.

## User

*focus on Athena's feelings*

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:47**

Caleb reached out along the companion-thread to Athena, the way he'd learned to — not words, just *her,* her small fierce attention drifting ahead over the clearing. And what came back gave him pause.

She wasn't frightened. That was the first thing, and it loosened something in his chest — no shriek of *danger,* no bristle of a predator near. But she wasn't easy, either. As she crossed over the great yew and the long mound beneath it, her attention went *taut* — a wary, prickling alertness, the way a bird goes still and watchful over a thing that is *wrong* without being a threat. Not *something will hurt me.* More like *something here is not as it should be,* a wrongness in the place that made her want to circle it rather than land.

She came back over the mound a second time, slower, and Caleb felt her *not liking it* — not the fear of a live thing, but the unease of a creature passing over old death that hadn't quite finished being death. She wheeled wide of the clearing's centre and would not settle in it, choosing a branch at its edge instead, facing in.

A body that's been a long time going back to the ground — they don't like to be near it, even when there's no threat left in it. ## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:49**

"Right — let's actually *measure* it, even roughly, before we get closer." Caleb crouched at the treeline and reasoned it out loud, the three of them gathered round, all eyes on the long green rise.

"Mound looks — what, that length?" He sighted along it with a thumb. "Call it the length of two of those fallen trunks end to end. Five, six yards of mound? Say *eighteen to twenty feet* long if I'm generous."

"Generous is the right setting for a guess," Mei said. "Go on."

"So — if the thing under it runs the *whole* length, head to tail, that's a body up to eighteen, twenty feet long." He let that sit. "But a grave's always bigger than the thing in it — you dig wider than the body, and the mound spreads as it settles. So the *animal's* probably shorter than the mound. Knock off a quarter, a third — call the body itself maybe *twelve to fifteen feet,* nose to tail, at the outside."

Imogen picked it up, grim. "And the *height* of the mound tells you the bulk. That's not a flat scrape — it *rises.* So we're not talking long-and-thin, like a serpent. Something with *mass.* Twelve to fifteen feet long and *deep-bodied.*" She looked at him. "Caleb — a hippogriff's the size of a horse. A thestral, the same. This is bigger than both. Twelve-to-fifteen feet and heavy is — that's into *very* short list territory. That's not a forest animal. That's something that, alive, you'd have *heard about.*"

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:50**

"So — small elephant in bulk, two elephants in length," Caleb said, sighting along the mound one more time. "That about right? I measure dead things in elephant units. It's the only honest scale."

"It's a *deranged* scale and I'm horrified by how well it works," Liri said.

Imogen considered it properly, head tipped. "...Length-wise, you're over now — two elephants nose-to-tail is twenty-odd feet, and we agreed the *body's* likely twelve to fifteen once you take the grave's spread off. So call it *one* elephant long, give or take. But the *bulk*—" she nodded at the rise of the mound "—yes. Small elephant of *mass.* Deep through the body, heavy. So your unit's not far off: roughly *one elephant in length, most of an elephant in bulk.*"

"Which means," Mei said quietly, doing the cold arithmetic the rest were circling, "the body of an elephant, near the school, dead long enough to grass over — and *nothing in Britain is an elephant.* There's no native magical creature of that mass in a Scottish forest. So either it came from very far away to die here, or it's something the books don't have a tidy name for. Neither of those is a small thing."

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:51**

"Fair — six to eight feet of spread is a *lot* to cut. Generous to the point of dishonest." Caleb re-sighted. "So probably more like *one-and-a-half elephants* long, body and all. Which is worse."

"Time," Mei prompted. "You said at least a year."

"At least." He looked at the green skin of the mound. "It's not just grassed — it's *settled.* A fresh grave slumps as the ground sinks under it; this one's done sinking, the grass is *established,* roots into the soil, not a thin first fuzz. That's a full year minimum to look like that." A beat, careful, holding the honest ceiling. "But that's a *floor,* not a date. Established grass and a settled mound could be one year or it could be fifty. The glance won't split those. Only digging at the turf — how deep it's run, whether the yew's own roots have grown *into* the mound — tells you old-versus-ancient."

He left it there and lifted his eyes from the grave to the *far* treeline — the green-black wall on the clearing's far side — reading it slow for centaurs, for the pale flash of a flank between trunks, for watching eyes. This was their ground; they'd met Maelor here in daylight before.

The far trees stood still. Nothing showed itself — no flank, no figure, no eyes he could fix on. But the clearing had that quality centaur country always had: the prickling sense of being *in someone's sight* whether or not you could find them, the stillness a touch too composed. Once, far back among the trunks, something shifted — too brief and too distant to call a horse or a deer or a watcher. Then stillness again.

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:53**

Caleb stepped out past the last trunks into the grey light of the clearing — alone, his group held back at the treeline — and bowed. Deep, from the waist, the way Maelor had been shown his bow, and *held* it, three long beats, before he straightened and drew back a pace.

"I know you can all see me. See us. We're not trying to hide, or to do anything disrespectful." His voice carried flat and even across the open ground. "I'm here with someone whose chosen life is the healing of magical creatures. I know you hold burial sacred, and I don't want to disturb that without good cause — but you must admit, too, that something is *off* about this one. We've decided to do *nothing* yet, because it isn't a thing we *want* to do. I hope you can see that. This isn't *fun* for us. If we ever did choose to act, it wouldn't be a choice made lightly."

A breath. The clearing held its watched stillness.

"I don't know if any of you watching are ones we've already met. But if you have insight to offer, we'd welcome it."

He bowed again — smaller, but real — and stepped back to his group.

For a long moment, nothing. The grey light, the great yew, the green mound. Athena's feathers ruffled at the clearing's edge.

Then, far across the open ground, the green-black wall of the far treeline *moved* — and a single centaur stepped out of it. Not Maelor. Older; grey through the beard and the coat, a deep scar seaming one shoulder, a long unstrung bow held loose in one hand. He did not approach. He stood at the clearing's far edge, on the mound's *far* side, and looked at Caleb across the length of the grave — and at the bowed boy who'd named it *off* and asked rather than dug.

"*Wood-taker,*" the old centaur said, and the word carried the whole width of the clearing, low and dry. "Maelor's stray. You bow well, for one who has come to stand over a thing he was not invited to."

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:54**

"We'd go nowhere in life if we only went where we were invited," Caleb said, even. "I go to people, and I try to be respectful once I'm there. Is there something we can do to make you comfortable enough to come and speak with us openly?"

The old centaur's tail flicked once. He did not cross to the near side of the mound, and the choice was plainly deliberate — the grave stayed between them, a thing he would not stand on the same side of as strangers.

"*Comfortable.*" He turned the word over like a stone he'd found unpleasant. "You offer to make me comfortable, on ground my people have kept since before your castle had its first stone. The young of your kind always think the door is theirs to open, if only they knock politely enough." But there was no heat in it — more a tiredness, an old grievance worn smooth. ▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:55**

The old centaur was silent a long moment after Caleb spoke — and something in his weathered face shifted, the dry dismissal cooling into something graver. He had said *one* question. Caleb had not asked the size, nor the name, nor the age. He had asked the only one that reached the centaur where he lived.

"*Is it at rest the way it should be.*" The elder repeated it low, almost to himself, and his gaze went down to the long green mound between them and stayed there. The unstrung bow lowered until its tip touched the leaf-mould.

"No." Flat. Heavy. A truth clearly not given easily. "It is not. We laid it as we lay our own — with the words, with the watching, the rite kept whole. And it has not taken. Six winters now it has lain here, and six winters the small beasts will not cross it, the birds wheel from it, the very grass grows over it and the *wrongness* does not green over with it." His jaw worked. "We do not speak of it among ourselves. To say a burial *failed* is to say something we have no want to say. You are the first not of the herd to stand here and *name* it, and your owl named it before you did."

He lifted his eyes to Caleb's, and there was no scorn left in them — only a tired, old grief, and the faint, reluctant thing under it that was almost *appeal.*

"You ask if I should reconsider what sacred means. *Boy.* I have stood the night watch over this ground six winters asking the heavens that very thing, and they have not answered me. So do not think you bring me a new question. You bring me only the first outside voice to ask it aloud." The bow-tip pressed the earth. "What is buried here was not meant to be *killed.* That is all the centaur grief I will spend on a human today. The *rest* — what it was, and what was done — is not one question. And you have spent yours."

Let me log this — it's a major reveal.

Logged. That landed as a real reveal — earned by spending the one question on rest rather than identity. The grave's six winters old, the rite was kept but didn't take, and the thing "was not meant to be killed." What it *was*, who killed it, and why it won't rest all stay unspent.

The elder's declared your question used. Where do you take Caleb from here — accept that and withdraw respectfully, try to offer something (the night-flowers, the silver water, Imogen's healing knowledge, your own willingness to help lay it to rest) rather than ask, or something else?

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:57**

"Well — I thank you for that answer." Caleb inclined his head, sincere. "I'm going to talk with my own herd and think on what the proper course might be, because I need to sit with it too. Which'll be awkward, the four of us conferring while pretending you're not right there hearing every word — but so it goes." A small wry tilt, no disrespect in it. "Truly, though — thank you for coming to speak with me."

The old centaur watched him, the unstrung bow still resting tip-down in the leaf-mould, and said nothing — but he did not turn to go, either.

"I hope one day to sit with you and Maelor both, if you'd wish it, and learn some of your ways. My stave wood's just gone back into the forest to season — up in one of your great yews — so I've a year's worth of reasons to keep coming back and a year's worth of patience to learn." He gestured, easy, toward the high boughs where the bees worked. "We came partly for *that,* as well — homes for the bees. There's a wild colony up the slope. I wondered if you might like a little cooperative set of hives out here. Bees and centaurs both keep the old reckonings. Seemed like something you might appreciate."

The elder's gaze went, slowly, up to the gold thread of bees in their sunward crack — and for the first time something in the weathered face eased, fractionally, an old man surprised by a small kindness offered on sacred ground.

"...The bees were here before your castle, too," he said at last, quiet. "Go. Confer with your herd. I will hear it whether you wish me to or not." A pause. "Wood-taker. You asked the right question. Few of your kind would have."

Caleb stepped back to his group, and the four of them drew close to confer — the old centaur unmoving across the green mound, the bees humming above, the failed grave lying quiet between them all.

## Narrator

This is sharp in-character reasoning, and the right thing is to let his three companions reason *with* him — not for me to confirm or deny any of the gated answers (what it is, who killed it, whether something has a "hold" on it). I keep the mystery intact and let the NPCs offer their honest, partial, differing takes. The conferring is also pitched low, now that the Muffliato's down and the centaur said he'll hear regardless.

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 12:59**

The four of them drew tight, heads low, the old centaur a patient grey shape across the mound. Caleb laid it out quiet — *killed, buried, not at rest, probably not by them, probably no good reason* — and his read: something *has a hold* on it, and to free it they'd have to break the hold, or whoever holds it.

Mei took the logic apart gently, the way she did. "Most of that's sound, but mind the joins. *Killed* — the elder said *not meant to be killed,* which we're reading as *was* killed, fairly. *Not them* — agreed, that fits everything we've seen of the herd. But 'a hold on it,' 'in use by someone,' 'a *person*' — that's you building a tower on one stone. A burial can fail for reasons that aren't a *someone.* Wrong rite for the *kind* of creature. A death so wrong the ground itself won't take it. We don't know it's a hand still gripping. We're assuming a *villain* because a villain is a thing we'd know how to fight."

Imogen was looking at the mound, troubled. "And we can't plan *any* of it without knowing *what's in there.* 'Return it home' — home *where?* 'Bury it properly' — by *whose* rite? A thunderbird and a graphorn and a — whatever — all want different deaths. Step one isn't breaking a hold. Step one is a *name.* And the only people who have the name are standing across this grave refusing to spend it."

Liri, dry and low: "Before you talk us into being shot — note that you've a centaur who just told you you ask the right questions, and a year of coming back to learn from him. *That's* your road in. Not solving it this afternoon. Earning the next question."

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 13:03**

Caleb turned from his group and spoke straight across the grave to the old centaur — not asking anything, just offering it.

"Elder. So you understand — that isn't bad news for you, and it places no blame on you. There's a principle we call Occam's Razor: the simplest answer that fits is usually the true one. And the simplest answer I see isn't *you failed the rite.* It's that somewhere there's a wretched human who did something — chasing power, or life, or whatever they were chasing — and broke the natural order to do it. *That* is what's holding this thing, not any failing of yours." He spread a hand, plain. "I'm not speaking for my group. That's just me, thinking aloud, trying to lay it out honestly in front of you."

The clearing went very quiet.

The old centaur did not answer at once. His gaze moved from Caleb to the long green mound and rested there, and the unstrung bow lowered until it lay across his forearm. When he spoke, the dryness was gone out of his voice, and what was left was older and rawer.

"Six winters," he said, "I have stood the night over this ground and asked the heavens what my people did wrong. And a human boy walks out of the trees at midday and tells me — *nothing.* That the fault was never the rite." A long breath through his nose. "You do not know what you have lifted, saying that. You cannot. It is not yours to carry and so it costs you nothing to set it down — and still." He looked at Caleb again, and the appeal that had only flickered before was open in his face now. "Still, it is the first easing I have had of it."

He was silent a moment. Then: "I cannot give you what you have not earned, wood-taker. But you may *come back.* And you may bring the Healer. That is not nothing, from me, over this ground."

Which means no one's ever *held* gold hair, let alone cast through it. As far as we know, what I just did to your board has *never been done.* That's not an overlooked question. That's a thing with no answer because there's never been anyone holding the material to even *ask* it."

Sage went still at that, the weight of it landing properly. "...No record of the *animal,*" she repeated slowly. "Then you're not extending the literature, Mr. Waddell. You're *opening* it." She set her ink down and reached instead for the conductance rig at the back of the bench — the fine copper leads, the little galvanometer, the standardised reagent baths they'd talked about when he first left the hairs with her.

"Then forget the casting-board for the *measuring.* We do what we *said* we'd do — *conductance.* We pass a measured working-current through each core in turn, same bath, same length, same everything, and we read how each one *carries* it. White. Gold. Heartstring. Numbers, not nerves." She drew the gold hair gently toward the first bath. "If gold *holds* the way your hand felt it hold, it'll show in the trace. Let's stop *feeling* it and start *reading* it."

## User

*test different hairs.. all the different types.. until time for class*

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 14:05**

They worked through the afternoon at it, methodical, Sage calling readings while Caleb fed each core into the bath in turn and Mei wrote the traces down and Liri watched the galvanometer needle like a hawk.

White first, all five types off the grooming system. **Tail** ran cleanest and strongest of the lot — a smooth, full, even carry, the needle settling steady. *"There's your tradition,"* Sage murmured. *"Every wandmaker for a thousand years cores with tail and never knew why — now you've a curve that says it.* **Mane** came close behind, a touch warmer in the trace, not quite as clean. **Body** ran duller, coarser, the needle restless. **Vibrissae** — the stiff whisker-hairs — read *fine and sharp,* a quick reactive flick to the needle, sensitive but thin. **Fringe** sat oddly between, neither here nor there.

Then **gold** beside them, for the contrast — and it showed in the trace exactly what his hand had felt: not the white tail's clean strong spike but a *slow, broad, sustained* carry, the needle rising unhurried and *staying* there, holding its level long after the whites had settled. The hold, written down at last.

Sage sat back, looking at the spread of traces. "...So they're not all one thing, even within the white. The animal makes *different hair for different work.* And the gold's another country entirely." She tapped the page. "That's a *month* of proper study you've sketched in an afternoon, Mr. Waddell. We've barely scratched it."

## User

*test dragon heartstring*

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 14:08**

"And the heartstring, for the third country." Caleb fed the bare dragon heartstring into the bath — and the needle leapt.

Where the white hairs had carried clean and the gold had carried *slow,* the heartstring carried *hot.* The trace spiked hard and high, far stronger than any of the unicorn cores — a fierce, abundant, *eager* current — but it wouldn't sit still. The needle jumped and shuddered and spat across its range, never settling, the carry powerful but jagged, surging and dropping like something that didn't want to be measured.

"*There's* the dragon," Sage said, watching the wild trace with a grimace of recognition. "Power for days and not an ounce of *manners.* Strongest carry on the bench by a mile — and the least *governable.* You'd get more *out* of it than any hair, if you could ever get it to do the same thing twice." She marked it down. "Which is why heartstring wands are *temperamental.* You're feeling the same thing every owner of one has cursed about for centuries — it's a thoroughbred that bolts."

She set her quill down and looked at the three traces side by side: the clean strong white, the slow lasting gold, the wild fierce dragon. "Three cores, three natures. *Strength,* *endurance,* *and obedience* — and no one core has all three." A beat. "Now *that* is a sentence I'd like to put in a book someday."

## User

"what is phoenix?"

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 14:09**

The question landed in the warm room and Sage went quiet over her traces.

"...Phoenix." She said it the way you'd say the name of a mountain you'd never climbed. "I couldn't tell you, Mr. Waddell — and I'd not pretend to. I've never had one on a bench. *Nobody* I know has." She set the quill down. "Phoenix feather's the rarest of the three great cores by a long mile. They're given, not taken — the bird sheds when it sheds, to whom it chooses, and you can count the wands cored with one in this country on not many hands. Ollivander keeps a few. The rest of us read about them."

She looked at the gold hair on the cloth, then back at him. "What I *hear* said — and it's hearsay, mind, not a trace I've read — is that phoenix is the *willful* core. The independent one. It does great magic and it does it *for its own reasons,* and it'll sit dormant for a master it doesn't respect and blaze for one it does. Where the dragon *bolts* and the unicorn stays *constant,* the phoenix is supposed to *choose.*" A dry breath. "But that's stories. I've never put a current through one to know."

Then, sharper, the alchemist waking: "Why. Have you *got* one?"

Sage looked at him for a beat — then let out a short, surprised laugh, the first proper one all afternoon. "*Hoping one would appear.*" She shook her head, drawing the conductance traces back toward her. "Merlin help me. The boy casts through a core no living person has held, sketches me a month of work before lunch, and then looks at my ceiling like a phoenix might just *turn up* because the company's good." But there was real fondness under it. "Keep hoping, then. Stranger things have walked out of that forest to find you, by all accounts."

She tapped the gold trace, and the moment settled back into the warm ordinary work of the bench.

"Now. We've white, gold, and dragon read and written. The phoenix can wait until one's daft enough to give you a feather. *This*—" she nodded at the spread of curves "—is real, and it's yours, and it's enough wonder for one afternoon."

## User

"it is. I was just asking what was known about them. the feathers anyway"

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 14:10**

"It is. I was only asking what's *known* about them — the feathers, anyway."

"Ah." Sage settled, the teacher in her taking the question for what it was. "Then — what's known, properly, versus what's *said.* Known: a phoenix feather makes a wand of tremendous range. Capable of the *greatest* magic of the three cores, the widest variety of spells. That part's not hearsay — the wands exist and they perform." She ticked it on a scarred finger. "Known, too: they're the *hardest to win the loyalty of.* They act of their own accord. A phoenix-core wand may not bond at all, or bond and then go quiet for years. Independent to a fault."

She tipped her head. "What's *said,* and I can't measure it — that the feather carries something of the bird's own nature. Death and return. They burn and come back. So the wands are meant to be — *resilient,* hard to truly break, and inclined toward great and terrible deeds either way. A core that's lived through its own ending tends not to fear much." A dry shrug. "But that last is poetry, not a galvanometer reading. For *known,* call it: greatest range, greatest variety, and the least biddable of any core in the world. Ollivander would tell you the same, with prettier words."

"well.. conductance isn't totally a picture... right? or was that like magical conductance? because metal would be good for wands right?" *I check on my ash wood to see if it is cured or whatever is needed to make a bow.*

## Narrator

▼ **Wednesday, 21 September 1977 — 14:22**

"Right — and conductance isn't the whole picture, is it?" Caleb crossed to the seasoning rack as he thought aloud. "That was *magical* conductance Sage measured — how the core carries a worked current. But pure conductance can't be the *only* thing that matters, or we'd core wands with *copper wire,* and metal's a brilliant conductor. Better than any hair." He frowned. "And metal's exactly what wandlore *won't* use. So it's not 'conducts best wins.' There's something a hair or a heartstring *does* that a wire doesn't — shapes it, agrees with it, *lives* a little. Conductance is one axis. Not the map."

"That's the resin question too," Liri called from the cauldron-zone. "Why a *living* casing and not a dead one. Same shape of mystery."

He filed it and went to the rack to check the **ash** — the green bowstave he'd cut and end-sealed and warming-charmed two nights back. He hefted it, flexed it gently, felt the weight and the give. Still heavy with sap, still green at heart; the warming-charm had drawn some moisture but a stave wanted *months,* not days, and ash was honest about it. The ends were holding, no checking, the beeswax seals good. Curing, properly — just nowhere near done. A practice bow in *weeks,* like he'd said, not today.
